Introduction to

Poems and Sonnets

Shakespeare became famous as a poet before most people knew that he also wrote plays. To judge by the frequency of admiring allusions and demand for printed copies, Venus and Adonis was the most popular long poem of the Elizabethan age. Like Shakespeare’s subsequent narrative poem, The Rape of Lucrece , it was written during the period in 1593–94 when the theatres were closed due to plague, and based on the works of the Roman poet Ovid. These works are calling cards which announce Shakespeare’s poetic sophistication, perhaps in response to the jibe in Greene’s Groatsworth of Wit (1592) about ‘Shake-scene’, the ‘upstart crow’, the vulgar jack-of-all-trades from the country.

Great swathes of Venus and Adonis are composed in the form of dialogue, while the eye contact between male and female subjects is self-consciously theatricalized. In both their speech patterns and their accompanying actions, Venus and Adonis are turned into dramatic characters, their story into a theatrical encounter, albeit one that relies on a naturalistic rural setting peopled with animals and natural forces that could not have been represented on stage. The Elizabethan reader’s pleasure in the poem lay in its cunning rhetoric, the inventive conceit of its language. The resourceful Venus has many an example, as when she compares herself to a park and Adonis to a deer. The double entendre whereby landscape and body parts become as one is typical of the poem: Shakespeare is trying out that language of indefatigable innuendo that will characterize so many of his subsequent plays. He is also experimenting with the idea that sexual attraction is sparked by contrariness and apparent disdain. The dynamic between a pair of erotically-charged horses anticipates not only the relationship of Venus and Adonis, but also those of the speaker of the sonnets and his beloved, not to mention Berowne and Rosaline in Love’s Labour’s Lost and Beatrice and Benedick in Much Ado about Nothing .

For most of Venus and Adonis , sexual desire is a source of comedy, whereas in The Rape of Lucrece the story of sexual pursuit is replayed in a darker key. Having made a comic spectacle of the rapacious goddess, Shakespeare makes a tragic spectacle of the raped emblem of chastity. The two poems are opposite sides of the same coin, as may be seen from their structural resemblance: in each, an ardent suitor attempts to gain the reluctant object of her/his sexual desire by means of rhetorical persuasion, fails, and indirectly or directly precipitates the death of the object of desire. Both poems are centrally interested in the way in which linguistic art is instrumental in the pursuit of sexual satisfaction. Nothing provokes desire more than antithesis. The more artless Lucrece is, the more Tarquin wishes to exercise the arts of love upon her.

The Rape of Lucrece is not only Shakespeare’s most sustained imitation of a classical source, it is also a supreme example of the art of ‘copiousness’ that was recommended by sixteenth-century humanist literary theorists: Shakespeare expanded the seventy-three lines of the Lucrece story in Ovid’s Fasti into nearly two thousand. As with Venus and Adonis , the most significant elaborations are those that invest the characters with linguistic arts. Three extended discourses are introduced: Tarquin’s inward disquisition as to whether he should carry through his desire, the disputation between the two characters in the bedroom, and Lucrece’s formal ‘complaint’ after the rape. At the end of the poem, the final consequence of the rape is played through as the Tarquins are heaved off their throne and the Roman republic is established. It is because loss of empire is the ultimate cost which Tarquin pays for his conquest – the military metaphors are all-pervasive – that his victory is a defeat. The oxymoronic structure of the narrative is thus brought to a climax: Tarquin’s gain is his family’s loss, Lucrece’s loss is Rome’s gain. She is the sacrificial victim required for the bringing of a new political order.

Shakespeare was much possessed by death, even when – as in Venus and Adonis and the comedies – he wrote in the genres of life and love. Venus expostulates against Death because he is the destroyer of beauty. She proposes to Adonis that the beautiful have a duty to reproduce themselves, not to hoard their loveliness in the manner of a miser (or, in the bawdy sub-text, a masturbator). The first seventeen of Shakespeare’s sonnets are a set of variations on the same theme. Throughout the entire collection of 154 sonnets, there is a frequent return to questions first explored in Venus and Adonis : not only mortality and endurance, beauty and its transience, but also the paradoxes of self and other, truth and delusion, in the dynamics of desire. Adonis’ eyes are ‘Two glasses’ (i.e. mirrors) where Venus ‘herself herself beheld / A thousand times.’ At the climax of the sonnet sequence, a key pun plays on the same idea: ‘For I have sworn thee fair: more perjured eye, / To swear against the truth so foul a lie.’ The relationship between ‘I’ and ‘eye’, inner self and the object of the gaze, is an obsession in the sonnets, while ‘perjured’ is an example of another hallmark of the collection, the application of legal language to the promises made and broken by lovers. So, for instance, in Sonnet 30 (‘When to the sessions of sweet silent thought’), term after term has legal connotations (‘sessions’, ‘summon’, ‘dateless’, ‘cancelled’, ‘expense’, ‘grievances’, ‘account’, ‘restored’).

It is probable that Shakespeare began composing sonnets soon after writing Venus and Adonis –several are woven into Romeo and Juliet and Love’s Labour’s Lost , plays that he wrote soon after the theatres reopened in 1594, and others circulated in manuscript by 1598. But the collection entitled Shakespeare’s Sonnets was not published until 1609. We know neither whether it was authorized nor whether its arrangement of the sequence was purposeful, though some clearly belong together, in that successive sonnets sometimes allude to each other or enact variations on a similar theme.

As one would expect from the hand of a dramatist, there does seem to be a plot-line running through the sequence and a ‘character’ to each of the personae. The first 126 poems appear to be written to a man (or a succession of men?). The narrative extends over a considerable period of time and runs the full gamut of emotions. The person addressed is younger than Shakespeare and of higher rank. He is lovely and the image of his mother. The first seventeen poems are exhortations to breed, in the manner of Venus’ address to Adonis: ‘From fairest creatures we desire increase, / That thereby beauty’s rose might never die.’ There is then a modulation towards the idea that the poet’s own work of praise may enable the young man to escape the ravages of time and death. Some sort of relationship is then imagined, with the youth in a position of power and the poet in one of supplication. Absence, travel, ‘disgrace’, melancholy, estrangement and reunion are variously implied. The young man appears to have an affair with the poet’s mistress, thus abusing the bond of friendship. But he is eventually forgiven: ‘Take all my loves, my love, yea, take them all: / What hast thou then more than thou hadst before?’ Later, the poet is discomposed by a rival who claims to have been taught by spirits to write ‘above a mortal pitch’ and who, with ‘the proud full sail of his great verse’, wins the patronage of the fair youth. The sequence ends with its key motif of the battle between love and time. The final poem to the youth is two lines short of the sonnet form’s customary fourteen. It ends with a pair of empty brackets, signalling some kind of closure or lacuna.

Sonnets 127–52, by contrast, explore the poet’s relationship with a mistress, a dark-complexioned and sexually voracious woman who has ‘raven black’ brows. Sometimes her dark beauty is wittily defended against the blonde Elizabethan ideal, but more frequently these poems are filled with self-abnegation, misogyny, a lingering sense of the sour taste that comes after sex, and disgust at the way in which the body rules the spirit. The woman is accused of infidelity, including an apparent affair with the ‘man right fair’ who is the poet’s ‘better angel’ – this seems to allude back to the purported relationship between ‘friend’ and ‘mistress’ in the earlier sequence. Some of the ‘dark lady’ poems are seeringly honest about the deceptions that may occur between lovers (‘O, love’s best habit is in seeming trust … Therefore I lie with her and she with me, / And in our faults by lies we flattered be’), while others are dazzlingly playful (notably 135–6, with their multiple punning on senses of the word ‘Will’, including a persistent play on Shakespeare’s own name). The final two sonnets are imitations of a Greek epigram about the fire of love being quenched in a cool well, with clear allusion to the Elizabethan custom of taking mercury baths as a cure for syphilis. The implicit suggestion is that the poet has been venereally infected by the ‘dark lady’.

Shakespeare’s sonnets are a source of endless biographical fascination because they seem to be the one work in which he speaks in his own voice. ‘Scorn not the sonnet’, William Wordsworth wrote two centuries later: ‘With this key, Shakespeare unlocked his heart.’ So it is that the sonnets are often believed to bear a wholly different relationship to Shakespeare’s biography from that of the rest of his literary work. There is, however, no intrinsic reason why a sonnet – a highly artificial literary form – should not be a dramatic performance just as a play is. It may perfectly well be argued that for an Elizabethan poet to dash off a sequence of sonnets was a kind of exercise, a proof of artistic skill akin to the work of a composer writing a set of variations on a musical theme. If Shakespeare could imagine Hamlet and Romeo and Viola, he could also have invented the ‘plot’ and ‘characters’ of his sonnets. Robert Browning responded to Wordsworth’s claim: ‘If so, the less Shakespeare he!’ Maybe the sonnets are best read as assays of Shakespeare’s art, demonstrations of his seemingly effortless verbal facility.

Unlike several contemporary sonneteers, Shakespeare does not name names. Because he is so guarded, the circumstances of composition have provoked centuries of speculation. The young man to whom the bulk of the poems are addressed may or may not be synonymous with the mysterious ‘Mr W. H.’ named in the collection’s dedication. The traditional candidates for the role of addressee are the Earl of Pembroke and the Earl of Southampton, though neither of them was a ‘Mr’. A very strong case has been made for the possibility that ‘Mr W. H.’ is actually a misprint for ‘Mr W. S.’ and that in the dedication Thomas Thorpe, the publisher, is merely acknowledging Shakespeare as the ‘only begetter’ of the sonnets (‘begetting’ was a common metaphor for authoring).

Dozens of male Elizabethan poets wrote sonnet sequences, but only Shakespeare and Richard Barnfield addressed their poems explicitly to a man. Barnfield wrote in the explicitly homoerotic tradition of ancient Greek pastoral poetry, whereas Shakespeare’s sequence emphasizes the spiritual aspects of the poet’s love for the fair youth. The only sonnets in the collection where ‘Will’ is actually in bed with a lover are addressed to the dark lady. The young man’s ‘thing’ (which has been ‘pricked out’ by nature) is, says the poet in Sonnet 20, ‘to my purpose nothing’ – though this is supremely ambiguous, since it could mean either that he is not interested in a physical relationship or that the prick serves him in the same way as a woman’s ‘nothing’ (vagina). Taken in their entirety, the sonnets associate heterosexual desire with consummation and disgust, homoerotic attraction with spirituality and an intensity that derives in large measure from the impossibility of consummation. Tempting as it may be to infer Shakespeare’s sexuality from this duality, it might be better to read the opposition between dark lady and fair youth as a dramatic device: one is a ‘character’ representing desire in its sexual manifestation, the other in its idealizing and spiritual.

Several short poems of the period have been attributed to Shakespeare, but in only two cases is the attribution absolutely secure. One is a beautifully turned epilogue that meditates on time in an address to Queen Elizabeth at the end of a court performance in February 1599. It remained unknown in manuscript until the late twentieth century. The other is the mysterious poem that has become generally known as ‘The Phoenix and Turtle’ (though Shakespeare did not give it a title).

In 1601, a minor author called Robert Chester published a long allegorical poem entitled Love’s Martyr , in which the mythical phoenix, representing Queen Elizabeth, accepts the devoted service of a turtle-dove, symbolizing a loyal courtier. The poem was dedicated to John Salusbury of Llewenni, Denbighshire, and seems to have been printed in honour of his being knighted by the queen in June 1601. Salusbury is therefore a prime candidate for the role of the turtle-dove. When the poem was published, it was accompanied by ‘Diverse Poetical Essays on the Former Subject; viz. The Turtle and Phoenix’, also dedicated to Salusbury and dated 1601. One of these poetical essays, beginning ‘Let the bird of loudest lay’, was signed ‘William Shakespeare’ and is unmistakably in his style. The poem seems to be a response to the closing sequence of Chester’s: it imagines a group of birds at the funeral of the phoenix and turtle. Again, the phoenix seems to be Elizabeth, the turtle a loyal subject: ‘Distance and no space was seen / ’Twixt this turtle and his queen.’ But ‘Distance and no space’ is more than a metaphor for the combination of propriety and loyalty required by good courtiership. It is also a metaphysical conundrum. On being invited (and presumably paid) to contribute to Chester’s volume, Shakespeare took the opportunity to rework the union of phoenix and turtle as a philosophical tour de force instead of a piece of routine poetic flattery. A line such as ‘Either was the other’s mine’ may allude back to a lengthy and tedious section of Chester’s poem concerning different kinds of mining, but it works primarily as a brilliant pun on the exchange of selfhood that is the core of true love. Whereas Chester’s ambition was to gain or retain Salusbury’s patronage, Shakespeare rose above the occasion and indulged a vein of serious intellectual play of a kind much closer to the dazzling mind-bending of John Donne. ‘Single natures, double name, / Neither two nor one was called’: these paradoxes could serve not only as an epitaph for the phoenix and the turtle, but also as an epigraph for the double vision – the ‘I am not that I am’, the seeing ‘with parted eye, / When everything seems double’ – that shapes Shakespeare’s dramatic universe.

KEY FACTS

LINGUISTIC MEDIUM: Venus and Adonis is in a six-line stanza rhyming ababcc , Lucrece in a seven-line stanza rhyming ababbcc (known as ‘rhyme royal’), both staple metres for poetic romance. Shakespeare’s sonnets, like most other English examples of the period, are shaped as three quatrains and a couplet (typically rhyming ababcdcdefefgg ), in contrast to the Petrarchan or Italian structure of an octave and a sestet. ‘Let the Bird of Loudest Lay’ and ‘To the Queen’ are both written in trochaic tetrameters (as is, for example, the Fairies’ song at the end of A Midsummer Night’s Dream ).

DATE: Venus and Adonis was published in 1593, The Rape of Lucrece in 1594, ‘To the Queen’ was written for a court performance on 20 February 1599. ‘Let the Bird of Loudest Lay’ was commissioned for a book published in 1601. The date of the sonnets is much disputed: the published volume was registered for publication in May 1609, but the vogue for sonneteering was at its height around the 1592–94 closure of the theatres due to plague. According to Francis Meres, some of Shakespeare’s ‘sugared sonnets’ were circulating ‘among his private friends’ in manuscript by 1598 (variant manuscript texts exist for several of them). Sonnet 107 apparently alludes to Queen Elizabeth’s death (spring 1603). Analysis of rare words suggests that 1–103 and 127–54 may date from the 1590s, 104–26 from the early 1600s.

SOURCES: Venus and Adonis is based on a story in book 10 of Ovid’s Metamorphoses , with some use of other Ovidian stories such as those of the lovely boys Narcissus (book 3) and Hermaphroditus (book 4). Lucrece derives from book 2 of Ovid’s Fasti and perhaps a translation from Livy’s History in William Painter’s Pallace of Pleasure (1566). The sonnets draw on, but also parody, an array of sonneteering conventions in the tradition that goes back to Petrarch; Ovidian interests such as desire and narcissism, time and change, and the durability of poetry are also pervasive.

TEXT: The well-printed 1593 Quarto of Venus and Adonis was reprinted in 1594, 1595?, 1596, 1599, 1599, 1602?, 1602, 1602, 1617, making it by a considerable margin Shakespeare’s bestseller in print. The well-printed 1594 Quarto of Lucrece was a little less popular but still much in demand (reprinted 1598, 1600, 1600, 1607, 1616). ‘To the Queen’ remained in manuscript until 1972. ‘Let the Bird of Loudest Lay’ was included in LOVES MARTYR OR, ROSALINS COMPLAINT. Allegorically shadowing the truth of Loue, in the constant Fate of the Phoenix and Turtle (1601), a verse collection dedicated to Sir John Salusbury, which included work by John Marston, George Chapman and Ben Jonson appended to a long allegorical poem by the little-known Robert Chester; Shakespeare’s contribution is untitled and only became generally known as ‘The Phoenix and Turtle’ (i.e. turtle-dove) from 1807. SHAKE-SPEARES SONNETS. Never before Imprinted was published in 1609, with ‘A Louers complaint. BY WILLIAM SHAKE-SPEARE’ filling up the final leaves (not included in this edition). Littered with printing errors, it was little noticed upon publication and not reprinted. In 1640, John Benson published a collection of Poems: Written by Wil. Shake-speare. Gent ., mainly based on the 1609 volume, but with considerable additions and alterations. The sonnets and poems did not enter the tradition of ‘Complete Works’ Shakespeare until Edmond Malone edited them for his supplement to the 1778 Samuel Johnson/George Steevens edition.

EXCLUSIONS: The Passionate Pilgrim , a tiny volume containing 20 sonnets and song lyrics, was attributed to Shakespeare on publication in late 1598 or 1599. It is a mix of five poems by Shakespeare (two of the sonnets, and three lyrics from Love’s Labour’s Lost ), some by others and some of uncertain authorship. We exclude it, since the Shakespearean poems appear elsewhere in our Complete Works , albeit in variant texts. ‘A Lover’s Complaint’ was published with the sonnets and attributed to Shakespeare, but his authorship has often been doubted, and recent scholarship (notably Brian Vickers, Shakespeare, ‘A Lover’s Complaint’ and John Davies of Hereford , 2007) has devastated the claim to Shakespearean authenticity and made a strong case for the authorship of John Davies of Hereford (1565?–1618), an admirer and imitator of Shakespeare. Various other short poems, notably several epigrams and epitaphs, have early attributions to Shakespeare, but none sufficiently secure to merit inclusion. Edited and annotated texts of The Passionate Pilgrim and ‘A Lover’s Complaint’ are downloadable from our website, www.rscshakespeare.co.uk.

Venus and Adonis

Vilia miretur vulgus; mihi flavus Apollo

Pocula Castalia plena ministret aqua

TO THE RIGHT HONOURABLE HENRY WRIOTHESLEY 2 , EARL

OF SOUTHAMPTON AND BARON OF TITCHFIELD

RIGHT HONOURABLE,

I KNOW not how I shall offend in dedicating my unpolished lines to your lordship, nor how the world will censure me for choosing so strong a prop to support so weak a burden; only if your honour seem but pleased, I account myself highly praised and vow to take advantage of all idle hours 3 , till I have honoured you with some graver 4 labour 5 . But if the first heir of my invention 6 prove deformed, I shall be sorry it had so noble a godfather and never after ear 7 so barren a land, for fear it yield me still so bad a harvest. I leave it to your honourable survey 8 , and your honour to your heart’s content, which I wish may always answer your own wish and the world’s hopeful expectation.

Your honour’s in all duty,

William Shakespeare

EVEN as 9 the sun with purple-coloured 10 face

Had ta’en his last leave of the weeping morn,

Rose-cheeked Adonis hied him 11 to the chase 12 .

Hunting he loved, but love he laughed to scorn.

Sick-thoughted 13 Venus makes amain 14 unto him

And like a bold-faced suitor ’gins to woo him.

‘Thrice-fairer than myself’, thus she began,

’The field’s chief flower, sweet above compare,

Stain to 15 all nymphs 16 , more lovely 17 than a man,

More white and red 18 than doves or roses are:

Nature that made thee, with herself at strife,

Saith 19 that the world hath ending with thy life.

’Vouchsafe 20 , thou wonder, to alight 21 thy steed

And rein his proud 22 head to the saddle-bow 23 .

If thou wilt deign this favour, for thy meed 24

A thousand honey secrets 25 shalt thou know:

Here come and sit, where never serpent hisses,

And being set 26 , I’ll smother thee with kisses.

’And yet not cloy thy lips with loathed satiety 27 ,

But rather famish them amid their plenty,

Making them red and pale with fresh variety:

Ten kisses short as one, one long as twenty.

A summer’s day will seem an hour but short,

Being wasted 28 in such time-beguiling sport 29 .’

With this she seizeth on his sweating 30 palm,

The precedent of pith and livelihood 31 ,

And trembling in her passion, calls it balm 32 ,

Earth’s sovereign 33 salve to do a goddess good:

Being so enraged 34 , desire doth lend her force

Courageously 35 to pluck him from his horse.

Over one arm the lusty 36 courser 37 ’s rein,

Under her other was the tender 38 boy,

Who blushed and pouted in a dull disdain,

With leaden 39 appetite 40 , unapt 41 to toy 42 ,

She red and hot as coals of glowing fire,

He red for shame, but frosty in desire.

The studded 43 bridle on a ragged 44 bough

Nimbly she fastens. O, how quick is love!

The steed is stallèd up 45 , and even now

To tie the rider she begins to prove 46 :

Backward she pushed him, as she would 47 be thrust 48 ,

And governed him in strength though not in lust 49 .

So soon was she along as he was down 50 ,

Each leaning on their elbows and their hips:

Now doth she stroke his cheek, now doth he frown

And ’gins to chide 51 , but soon she stops his lips

And kissing speaks, with lustful language broken 52 ,

‘If thou wilt chide, thy lips shall never open.’

He burns with bashful shame, she with her tears

Doth quench the maiden 53 burning of his cheeks,

Then with her windy sighs and golden hairs

To fan and blow them dry again she seeks.

He saith she is immodest, blames her miss 54 :

What follows more, she murders with a kiss.

Even as an empty 55 eagle, sharp by fast 56 ,

Tires 57 with her beak on feathers, flesh and bone,

Shaking her wings, devouring all in haste,

Till either gorge 58 be stuffed or prey be gone:

Even so she kissed his brow, his cheek, his chin,

And where she ends, she doth anew begin.

Forced to content 59 , but never to obey,

Panting he lies and breatheth in her face.

She feedeth on the steam, as on a pray 60 ,

And calls it heavenly moisture, air of grace,

Wishing her cheeks were gardens full of flowers,

So they were dewed with such distilling 61 showers.

Look, how a bird lies tangled in a net,

So fastened in her arms Adonis lies.

Pure shame and awed 62 resistance made him fret 63 ,

Which bred more beauty in his angry eyes:

Rain added to a river that is rank 64

Perforce 65 will force it overflow the bank.

Still she entreats and prettily 66 entreats,

For to a pretty ear she tunes her tale.

Still is he sullen, still he lours 67 and frets,

’Twixt 68 crimson shame and anger ashy-pale:

Being red, she loves him best, and being white,

Her best is bettered with a more delight.

Look how he can 69 , she cannot choose but love,

And by her fair immortal hand she swears

From his soft bosom never to remove 70

Till he take truce with her contending 71 tears,

Which long have rained, making her cheeks all wet,

And one sweet kiss shall pay this countless 72 debt.

Upon this promise did he raise his chin,

Like a dive-dapper 73 peering through a wave,

Who, being looked on, ducks as quickly in:

So offers he to give what she did crave,

But when her lips were ready for his pay,

He winks 74 and turns his lips another way.

Never did passenger 75 in summer’s heat

More thirst for drink than she for this good turn 76 .

Her help she sees, but help she cannot get,

She bathes in water 77 , yet her fire must burn:

‘O, pity,’ ’gan she cry, ’flint-hearted boy!

’Tis but a kiss I beg, why art thou coy 78 ?

’I have been wooed, as I entreat thee now,

Even by the stern and direful god of war 79 ,

Whose sinewy 80 neck in battle ne’er did bow 81 ,

Who conquers where he comes in every jar 82 ,

Yet hath he been my captive and my slave

And begged for that which thou unasked shalt have.

’Over my altars hath he hung his lance,

His battered shield, his uncontrollèd 83 crest 84 ,

And for my sake hath learned to sport and dance,

To toy, to wanton, dally 85 , smile and jest,

Scorning his churlish 86 drum and ensign 87 red,

Making my arms 88 his field 89 , his tent my bed.

’Thus he that overruled I overswayed 90 ,

Leading him prisoner in a red rose chain.

Strong-tempered 91 steel his stronger strength obeyed 92 ,

Yet was he servile to my coy disdain.

O, be not proud, nor brag not of thy might,

For mast’ring her that foiled 93 the god of fight.

’Touch but my lips with those fair lips of thine —

Though mine be not so fair, yet are they red —

The kiss shall be thine own as well as mine.

What see’st thou in the ground? Hold up thy head.

Look in mine eyeballs, there thy beauty lies 94 ,

Then why not lips on lips, since eyes in eyes?

’Art thou ashamed to kiss? Then wink 95 again,

And I will wink, so shall the day seem night.

Love keeps his revels 96 where there are but twain 97 :

Be bold 98 to play, our sport is not in sight 99 .

These blue-veined 100 violets whereon we lean

Never can blab nor know not what we mean.

’The tender spring 101 upon thy tempting lip

Shows thee unripe; yet mayst thou well be tasted.

Make use of time, let not advantage slip,

Beauty within itself should not be wasted.

Fair flowers that are not gathered in their prime

Rot and consume themselves in little time.

’Were I hard-favoured 102 , foul 103 or wrinkled-old,

Ill-nurtured 104 , crooked 105 , churlish, harsh in voice,

O’erworn 106 , despisèd, rheumatic 107 and cold 108 ,

Thick-sighted 109 , barren, lean and lacking juice 110 ,

Then mightst thou pause, for then I were not for thee,

But having no defects, why dost abhor me?

’Thou canst not see one wrinkle in my brow,

Mine eyes are grey 111 and bright and quick in turning:

My beauty as the spring doth yearly grow,

My flesh is soft and plump, my marrow 112 burning,

My smooth moist hand, were it with thy hand felt,

Would in thy palm dissolve or seem to melt.

’Bid me discourse, I will enchant thine ear,

Or like a fairy trip 113 upon the green,

Or like a nymph 114 with long dishevelled hair

Dance on the sands and yet no footing 115 seen.

Love is a spirit 116 all compact 117 of fire,

Not gross 118 to sink, but light 119 and will aspire 120 .

’Witness this primrose bank whereon I lie,

These forceless 121 flowers like sturdy trees support me:

Two strengthless doves will draw me through the sky

From morn till night, even where I list 122 to sport me.

Is love so light 123 , sweet boy, and may it be

That thou should think it heavy 124 unto thee?

’Is thine own heart to thine own face affected 125 ?

Can thy right hand seize love upon thy left 126 ?

Then woo thyself, be of thyself rejected:

Steal thine own freedom and complain on 127 theft.

Narcissus 128 so himself himself forsook 129 ,

And died to kiss his shadow 130 in the brook.

’Torches are made to light, jewels to wear,

Dainties 131 to taste, fresh beauty for the use 132 ,

Herbs for their smell and sappy 133 plants to bear 134 .

Things growing to themselves are growth’s abuse:

Seeds spring from seeds and beauty breedeth beauty.

Thou wast begot: to get 135 it is thy duty.

’Upon the earth’s increase 136 why shouldst thou feed,

Unless the earth with thy increase be fed?

By law of nature thou art bound to breed,

That thine 137 may live when thou thyself art dead:

And so, in spite of death, thou dost survive,

In that thy likeness still is left alive.’

By this 138 the lovesick queen began to sweat,

For where they lay the shadow had forsook them,

And Titan 139 , tirèd 140 in the midday heat,

With burning eye did hotly 141 overlook them,

Wishing Adonis had his team 142 to guide,

So he were like him 143 and by Venus’ side.

And now Adonis with a lazy sprite 144

And with a heavy, dark, disliking 145 eye,

His louring brows o’erwhelming his fair sight 146

Like misty vapours 147 when they blot the sky,

Souring 148 his cheeks, cries, ’Fie, no more of love!

The sun doth burn my face. I must remove.’

‘Ay me,’ quoth Venus, ’young and so unkind,

What bare 149 excuses mak’st thou to be gone!

I’ll sigh celestial breath, whose gentle wind

Shall cool the heat of this descending sun:

I’ll make a shadow for thee of my hairs,

If they burn too, I’ll quench them with my tears.

’The sun that shines from heaven shines but warm,

And, lo, I lie between that sun and thee:

The heat I have from thence doth little harm,

Thine eye darts forth the fire that burneth me,

And were I not immortal, life were done 150

Between this heavenly and earthly sun 151 .

’Art thou obdurate, flinty, hard as steel?

Nay, more than flint, for stone at rain relenteth:

Art thou a woman’s son and canst not feel

What ’tis to love, how want 152 of love tormenteth?

O, had thy mother 153 borne so hard a mind,

She had not brought forth thee, but died unkind 154 .

’What am I that thou shouldst contemn 155 me this 156 ?

Or what great danger dwells upon my suit 157 ?

What were thy lips the worse for one poor kiss?

Speak, fair, but speak fair words or else be mute:

Give me one kiss, I’ll give it thee again,

And one for int’rest, if thou wilt have twain.

’Fie, lifeless picture, cold and senseless stone,

Well-painted idol, image dull and dead,

Statue 158 contenting but the eye alone,

Thing like a man, but of no woman bred:

Thou art no man, though of a man’s complexion 159 ,

For men will kiss even by their own direction 160 .’

This said, impatience chokes her pleading tongue,

And swelling passion doth provoke a pause,

Red cheeks and fiery eyes blaze forth 161 her wrong:

Being judge in love, she cannot right her cause 162 ;

And now she weeps and now she fain 163 would speak,

And now her sobs do her intendments 164 break.

Sometime she shakes her head and then his hand,

Now gazeth she on him, now on the ground;

Sometime her arms enfold him like a band,

She would 165 , he will not in her arms be bound:

And when from thence he struggles to be gone,

She locks her lily fingers one 166 in one.

‘Fondling 167 ,’ she saith, ’since I have hemmed thee here

Within the circuit of this ivory pale 168 ,

I’ll be a park 169 and thou shalt be my deer 170 :

Feed where thou wilt on mountain or in dale 171 ,

Graze on my lips and, if those hills be dry,

Stray lower, where the pleasant fountains lie.

Within this limit 172 is relief 173 enough,

Sweet bottom-grass 174 and high delightful plain 175 ,

Round rising hillocks 176 , brakes 177 obscure 178 and rough,

To shelter thee from tempest and from rain:

Then be my deer, since I am such a park,

No dog shall rouse 179 thee, though a thousand bark.’

At this Adonis smiles as in disdain,

That in each cheek appears a pretty dimple;

Love made those hollows, if 180 himself were slain,

He might be buried in a tomb so simple,

Foreknowing well, if there he came to lie,

Why, there love lived and there he could not die.

These lovely caves, these round enchanting pits 181 ,

Opened their mouths to swallow Venus’ liking:

Being mad before, how doth she now for wits 182 ?

Struck dead at first 183 , what needs a second striking?

Poor queen of love, in thine own law forlorn 184 ,

To love a cheek that smiles at thee in scorn.

Now which way shall she turn? What shall she say?

Her words are done, her woes are more increasing,

The time is spent, her object will away,

And from her twining arms doth urge releasing:

‘Pity!’ she cries, ‘Some favour, some remorse 185 !’

Away he springs and hasteth to his horse.

But, lo, from forth a copse that neighbours by 186 ,

A breeding 187 jennet 188 , lusty 189 , young and proud 190 ,

Adonis’ trampling courser 191 doth espy,

And forth she rushes, snorts and neighs aloud.

The strong-necked steed, being tied unto a tree,

Breaketh his rein and to her straight 192 goes he.

Imperiously he leaps, he neighs, he bounds,

And now his woven girths 193 he breaks asunder,

The bearing 194 earth with his hard hoof he wounds,

Whose hollow womb resounds like heaven’s thunder,

The iron bit he crusheth ’tween his teeth,

Controlling what he was controllèd with.

His ears up-pricked, his braided hanging mane

Upon his compassed crest 195 now stand on end,

His nostrils drink the air and forth again,

As from a furnace, vapours doth he send:

His eye, which scornfully glisters 196 like fire,

Shows his hot courage 197 and his high desire.

Sometime he trots, as if he told 198 the steps

With gentle 199 majesty and modest pride,

Anon 200 he rears upright, curvets 201 and leaps,

As who should 202 say, ’Lo, thus my strength is tried 203

And this I do to captivate the eye

Of the fair breeder that is standing by.’

What recketh he 204 his rider’s angry stir 205 ,

His flattering 206 ‘Holla 207’, or his ‘Stand, I say’?

What cares he now for curb 208 or pricking spur?

For rich caparisons 209 or trappings gay 210 ?

He sees his love and nothing else he sees,

For nothing else with his proud sight agrees.

Look when a painter would surpass the life

In limning out 211 a well-proportioned steed,

His art with nature’s workmanship at strife,

As if the dead the living should exceed:

So did this horse excel a common one

In shape, in courage, colour, pace and bone 212 .

Round-hoofed, short-jointed, fetlocks 213 shag 214 and long,

Broad breast, full eye, small head and nostril wide,

High crest 215 , short ears, straight legs and passing 216 strong,

Thin mane, thick tail, broad buttock, tender hide:

Look what a horse should have he did not lack,

Save a proud rider on so proud a back.

Sometime he scuds 217 far off and there he stares,

Anon he starts at stirring of a feather:

To bid the wind a base 218 he now prepares,

And where 219 he run or fly they know not whether:

For through his mane and tail the high wind sings,

Fanning the hairs, who 220 wave like feathered wings.

He looks upon his love and neighs unto her,

She answers him as if she knew his mind:

Being proud, as females are, to see him woo her,

She puts on outward strangeness 221 , seems unkind 222 ,

Spurns at 223 his love and scorns the heat he feels,

Beating his kind embracements 224 with her heels 225 .

Then, like a melancholy malcontent,

He vails 226 his tail 227 that like a falling plume 228

Cool shadow to his melting buttock lent.

He stamps and bites the poor flies in his fume 229 :

His love, perceiving how he was enraged,

Grew kinder and his fury was assuaged.

His testy 230 master goeth about to take him,

When, lo, the unbacked 231 breeder 232 , full of fear,

Jealous of catching 233 , swiftly doth forsake 234 him,

With her the horse, and left Adonis there:

As 235 they were mad, unto the wood 236 they hie them 237 ,

Outstripping crows that strive to overfly them.

All swoll’n with chafing 238 , down Adonis sits,

Banning 239 his boist’rous and unruly beast;

And now the happy season once more fits 240

That lovesick love 241 by pleading may be blest:

For lovers say the heart hath treble wrong

When it is barred the aidance 242 of the tongue.

An oven that is stopped 243 or river stayed 244

Burneth more hotly, swelleth with more rage:

So 245 of concealèd sorrow may be said

Free vent 246 of words love’s fire doth assuage,

But when the heart’s attorney 247 once is mute,

The client 248 breaks 249 , as 250 desperate in his suit 251 .

He sees her coming and begins to glow,

Even as a dying coal revives with wind,

And with his bonnet 252 hides his angry brow,

Looks on the dull earth with disturbèd mind,

Taking no notice that she is so nigh 253 ,

For all askance he holds her in his eye 254 .

O, what a sight it was, wistly 255 to view

How she came stealing to the wayward 256 boy,

To note the fighting conflict of her hue,

How white and red each other did destroy:

But 257 now her cheek was pale and by and by

It flashed forth fire, as lightning from the sky.

Now was she just before 258 him as he sat,

And like a lowly lover down she kneels,

With one fair hand she heaveth 259 up his hat,

Her other tender hand his fair cheek feels:

His tend’rer cheek receives her soft hand’s print,

As apt 260 as new-fall’n snow takes any dint 261 .

O, what a war of looks was then between them,

Her eyes petitioners to his eyes suing 262 ,

His eyes saw her eyes, as 263 they had not seen them,

Her eyes wooed still, his eyes disdained the wooing:

And all this dumb play 264 had his 265 acts made plain 266

With tears, which chorus-like 267 her eyes did rain.

Full 268 gently now she takes him by the hand,

A lily prisoned 269 in a jail 270 of snow,

Or ivory in an alabaster band 271 ,

So white a friend engirts 272 so white a foe:

This beauteous combat, wilful 273 and unwilling,

Showed like two silver doves that sit a-billing 274 .

Once more the engine 275 of her thoughts began,

’O fairest mover 276 on this mortal round 277 ,

Would thou wert 278 as I am, and I a man,

My heart all whole as thine, thy heart my wound 279 ,

For one sweet look thy help I would assure thee 280 ,

Though nothing but my body’s bane 281 would cure thee.’

‘Give me my hand,’ saith he, ‘why dost thou feel it?’

‘Give me my heart’, saith she, ’and thou shalt have it.

O, give it me, lest thy hard heart do steel 282 it,

And being steeled, soft sighs can never grave 283 it.

Then love’s deep groans I never shall regard 284 ,

Because Adonis’ heart hath made mine hard.’

‘For shame!’ he cries, ’Let go and let me go:

My day’s delight is past, my horse is gone,

And ’tis your fault I am bereft 285 him so.

I pray you hence 286 and leave me here alone,

For all my mind, my thought, my busy care,

Is how to get my palfrey 287 from the mare.’

Thus she replies, ’Thy palfrey, as he should,

Welcomes the warm approach of sweet desire:

Affection 288 is a coal that must be cooled,

Else, suffered 289 , it will set the heart on fire.

The sea hath bounds, but deep desire hath none,

Therefore no marvel though thy horse be gone.

’How like a jade 290 he stood, tied to the tree,

Servilely mastered with a leathern rein,

But when he saw his love, his youth’s fair fee 291 ,

He held such petty bondage in disdain,

Throwing the base thong 292 from his bending crest 293 ,

Enfranchising 294 his mouth, his back, his breast.

’Who sees his true-love in her naked bed 295 ,

Teaching the sheets a whiter hue than white,

But, when his glutton eye so full hath fed,

His other agents 296 aim at like 297 delight?

Who is so faint 298 that dares not be so bold 299

To touch the fire, the weather being cold?

’Let me excuse thy courser, gentle boy,

And learn of 300 him, I heartily beseech thee,

To take advantage on 301 presented 302 joy.

Though 303 I were dumb, yet 304 his proceedings 305 teach thee.

O, learn to love: the lesson is but plain,

And once made perfect 306 , never lost again.’

‘I know not love,’ quoth 307 he, ’nor will not know it,

Unless it be a boar and then I chase it,

’Tis much to borrow and I will not owe 308 it.

My love to love is love but to disgrace it 309 ,

For I have heard it is a life in death,

That laughs and weeps and all but with a breath 310 .

’Who wears a garment shapeless and unfinished?

Who plucks the bud before one leaf put forth?

If springing 311 things be any jot diminished 312 ,

They wither in their prime, prove nothing worth:

The colt that’s backed and burdened 313 being young

Loseth his pride and never waxeth 314 strong.

’You hurt my hand with wringing, let us part

And leave this idle 315 theme, this bootless 316 chat.

Remove your siege from my unyielding heart:

To love’s alarms 317 it will not ope 318 the gate.

Dismiss your vows, your feignèd tears, your flattery,

For where a heart is hard they make no battery 319 .’

‘What, canst thou talk?’ quoth she, ’Hast thou a tongue?

O, would thou hadst not, or I had no hearing.

Thy mermaid’s 320 voice hath done me double wrong:

I had my load before, now pressed 321 with bearing:

Melodious discord, heavenly tune harsh sounding,

Ear’s deep-sweet music and heart’s deep-sore wounding.

’Had I no eyes but ears, my ears would love

That inward beauty and invisible 322 ,

Or were I deaf, thy outward parts 323 would move 324

Each part in me that were but sensible 325 :

Though neither eyes nor ears, to hear nor see,

Yet should I be in love by touching thee.

’Say that the sense of feeling were bereft me,

And that I could not see nor hear nor touch,

And nothing but the very smell were left me,

Yet would my love to thee be still as much:

For from the stillatory 326 of thy face excelling 327

Comes breath perfumed that breedeth love by smelling.

’But O, what banquet wert thou to the taste,

Being nurse and feeder of the other four 328 .

Would they not wish the feast might ever last

And bid suspicion 329 double-lock the door,

Lest jealousy, that sour unwelcome guest,

Should by his stealing in disturb the feast?’

Once more the ruby-coloured portal 330 opened,

Which to his speech did honey passage yield,

Like a red morn that ever yet betokened

Wrack 331 to the seaman, tempest to the field,

Sorrow to shepherds, woe unto the birds,

Gusts and foul flaws 332 to herdmen and to herds.

This ill presage 333 advisedly 334 she marketh 335 ,

Even as the wind is hushed before it raineth,

Or as the wolf doth grin 336 before he barketh,

Or as the berry breaks before it staineth,

Or like the deadly bullet of a gun,

His meaning struck her ere 337 his words begun.

And at his look she flatly 338 falleth down,

For looks kill love and love by looks reviveth:

A smile recures 339 the wounding of a frown.

But blessèd bankrupt that by love 340 so thriveth 341 ,

The silly boy, believing she is dead,

Claps her pale cheek, till clapping makes it red,

And all amazed brake off his late intent 342 ,

For sharply he did think to reprehend her,

Which cunning 343 love did wittily 344 prevent:

Fair fall 345 the wit that can so well defend her!

For on the grass she lies as she were slain,

Till his breath breatheth life in her again.

He wrings 346 her nose, he strikes her on the cheeks,

He bends her fingers, holds her pulses hard,

He chafes her lips: a thousand ways he seeks

To mend the hurt that his unkindness marred.

He kisses her and she, by her good will,

Will never rise, so he will kiss her still 347 .

The night of sorrow now is turned to day.

Her two blue windows 348 faintly she upheaveth,

Like the fair sun when in his fresh array

He cheers the morn and all the earth relieveth:

And as the bright sun glorifies the sky,

So is her face illumined with her eye,

Whose beams upon his hairless face are fixed,

As if from thence they borrowed all their shine.

Were never four such lamps together mixed,

Had not his clouded with his brow’s repine 349 ,

But hers, which through the crystal tears gave light,

Shone like the moon in water seen by night.

‘O, where am I?’ quoth she, ’In earth or heaven,

Or in the ocean drenched 350 or in the fire?

What hour is this? Or 351 morn or weary even?

Do I delight to die or life desire?

But now I lived and life was death’s annoy 352 ,

But now I died and death was lively joy.

’O, thou didst kill me! Kill me once again!

Thy eyes’ shrewd 353 tutor, that hard heart of thine,

Hath taught them scornful tricks and such disdain

That they have murdered this poor heart of mine,

And these mine eyes, true leaders 354 to their queen,

But 355 for thy piteous 356 lips no more had seen.

’Long may they kiss each other for this cure!

O, never let their crimson liveries 357 wear 358 ,

And as they last, their verdure 359 still endure

To drive infection from the dangerous year

That the star-gazers 360 , having writ on death,

May say the plague 361 is banished by thy breath.

’Pure lips, sweet seals in my soft lips imprinted 362 ,

What bargains may I make, still to be sealing?

To sell myself 363 I can be well contented,

So 364 thou wilt buy and pay and use good dealing 365 ,

Which purchase if thou make, for fear of slips 366

Set thy seal-manual 367 on my wax-red lips.

’A thousand kisses buys my heart from me,

And pay them at thy leisure, one by one.

What is ten hundred touches 368 unto thee?

Are they not quickly told 369 and quickly gone?

Say for non-payment that the debt should double,

Is twenty hundred kisses such a trouble?’

‘Fair queen 370 ,’ quoth he, ’if any love you owe 371 me,

Measure my strangeness with 372 my unripe years,

Before I know 373 myself, seek not to know me.

No fisher but the ungrown fry 374 forbears 375 :

The mellow 376 plum doth fall, the green sticks fast,

Or being early plucked is sour to taste.

’Look, the world’s comforter 377 with weary gait

His day’s hot task hath ended in the west,

The owl, night’s herald, shrieks 378 , ’tis very late,

The sheep are gone to fold 379 , birds to their nest,

And coal-black clouds that shadow heaven’s light

Do summon us to part and bid good night.

‘Now let me say’‘Goodnight’’, and so say you:

If you will say so, you shall have a kiss.’

‘Goodnight’, quoth she and ere he says ‘Adieu’

The honey fee of parting tendered 380 is.

Her arms do lend his neck a sweet embrace:

Incorp’rate 381 then they seem, face grows to face,

Till, breathless, he disjoined 382 and backward drew

The heavenly moisture, that sweet coral mouth,

Whose precious taste her thirsty lips well knew,

Whereon they surfeit 383 , yet complain on drouth 384 :

He with her plenty 385 pressed 386 , she faint with dearth 387 ,

Their lips together glued, fall to the earth.

Now quick desire hath caught the yielding prey,

And glutton-like she feeds, yet never filleth.

Her lips are conquerors, his lips obey,

Paying what ransom the insulter 388 willeth,

Whose vulture 389 thought doth pitch 390 the price so high

That she will draw his lips’ rich treasure dry.

And having felt the sweetness of the spoil 391 ,

With blindfold fury she begins to forage 392 :

Her face doth reek 393 and smoke 394 , her blood doth boil,

And careless 395 lust stirs up a desperate courage,

Planting oblivion 396 , beating reason back,

Forgetting shame’s pure blush and honour’s wrack 397 .

Hot, faint and weary, with her hard embracing,

Like a wild bird being tamed with too much handling,

Or as the fleet-foot roe 398 that’s tired with chasing 399 ,

Or like the froward 400 infant stilled with dandling 401 ,

He now obeys and now no more resisteth,

While she takes all she can, not all she listeth 402 .

What wax so 403 frozen but dissolves with temp’ring 404 ,

And yields at last to every light impression?

Things out of hope 405 are compassed 406 oft with vent’ring 407 ,

Chiefly in love, whose leave exceeds commission 408 :

Affection faints not like a pale-faced coward,

But then woos best, when most his choice 409 is froward.

When he did frown, O, had she then gave over 410 ,

Such nectar from his lips she had not sucked.

Foul 411 words and frowns must not repel a lover:

What though the rose have prickles, yet ’tis plucked 412 !

Were beauty under twenty locks kept fast 413 ,

Yet love breaks through and picks them all at last.

For pity now she can no more detain him:

The poor fool prays her that he may depart.

She is resolved no longer to 414 restrain him,

Bids him farewell and look well to her heart,

The which, by Cupid 415 ’s bow she doth protest 416 ,

He carries thence incagèd in his breast.

‘Sweet boy,’ she says, ’this night I’ll waste 417 in sorrow,

For my sick heart commands mine eyes to watch 418 .

Tell me, love’s master, shall we meet tomorrow?

Say, shall we? Shall we? Wilt thou make the match 419 ?’

He tells her, ‘No’, tomorrow he intends

To hunt the boar with certain of his friends.

‘The boar?’ quoth she, whereat a sudden pale,

Like lawn 420 being spread upon the blushing rose,

Usurps her cheek: she trembles at his tale

And on his neck her yoking 421 arms she throws.

She sinketh down still hanging by his neck,

He on her belly falls, she on her back.

Now is she in the very lists 422 of love,

Her champion 423 mounted 424 for the hot encounter 425 :

All is imaginary she doth prove 426 ,

He will not manage 427 her, although he mount her,

That 428 worse than Tantalus 429 ’ is her annoy,

To clip 430 Elysium 431 and to lack her joy.

Even so poor birds, deceived with painted grapes 432 ,

Do surfeit by the eye and pine the maw 433 :

Even so she languisheth in her mishaps 434 ,

As those poor birds that helpless 435 berries saw.

The warm effects 436 , which she in him finds missing,

She seeks to kindle with continual kissing.

But all in vain: good queen, it will not be.

She hath assayed 437 as much as may be proved 438 .

Her pleading hath deserved a greater fee:

She’s love, she loves and yet she is not loved.

‘Fie 439 , fie!’ he says, ’You crush me! Let me go!

You have no reason to withhold me so.’

‘Thou hadst 440 been gone’, quoth she, ’sweet boy, ere this,

But that thou told’st me thou wouldst hunt the boar.

O, be advised 441 ! Thou know’st not what it is

With javelin’s point a churlish 442 swine to gore,

Whose tushes 443 never sheathed he whetteth still 444 ,

Like to a mortal 445 butcher bent 446 to kill.

’On his bow-back 447 he hath a battle 448 set

Of bristly pikes 449 that ever threat his foes,

His eyes like glow-worms shine when he doth fret 450 ,

His snout digs sepulchres 451 where’er he goes:

Being moved 452 , he strikes whate’er is in his way,

And whom he strikes, his crooked tushes slay.

’His brawny 453 sides with hairy bristles armed

Are better proof 454 than thy spear’s point can enter,

His short thick neck cannot be easily harmed,

Being ireful 455 on the lion he will venture 456 :

The thorny brambles and embracing bushes,

As fearful of him, part, through whom he rushes.

’Alas, he nought esteems that face of thine,

To which love’s eyes pays tributary 457 gazes,

Nor thy soft hands, sweet lips and crystal eyne 458 ,

Whose full perfection all the world amazes,

But having thee at vantage 459 — wondrous dread! —

Would root 460 these beauties as he roots the mead 461 .

’O, let him keep his loathsome cabin 462 still:

Beauty hath nought to do with such foul 463 fiends.

Come not within his danger by thy will 464 :

They that thrive well take counsel of their friends.

When thou didst name the boar, not to dissemble 465 ,

I feared thy fortune and my joints did tremble.

’Didst thou not mark my face? Was it not white?

Saw’st thou not signs of fear lurk in mine eye?

Grew I not faint? And fell I not downright 466 ?

Within my bosom whereon thou dost lie,

My boding 467 heart pants, beats and takes no rest,

But like an earthquake shakes thee on my breast.

’For where love reigns, disturbing jealousy 468

Doth call himself affection’s sentinel 469 ,

Gives false alarms, suggesteth 470 mutiny,

And in a peaceful hour doth cry ‘Kill, kill!’

Distemp’ring 471 gentle love in his desire,

As air and water do abate 472 the fire.

’This sour informer, this bate-breeding 473 spy,

This canker 474 that eats up love’s tender spring 475 ,

This carry-tale 476 , dissentious 477 jealousy,

That sometime true news, sometime false doth bring,

Knocks at my heart and whispers in mine ear

That if I love thee, I thy death should fear,

’And more than so 478 , presenteth to mine eye

The picture of an angry, chafing boar,

Under whose sharp fangs on his 479 back doth lie

An image like thyself, all stained with gore,

Whose blood upon the fresh flowers being shed

Doth make them droop with grief and hang the head.

’What should I do, seeing thee so indeed 480 ,

That 481 tremble at th’imagination?

The thought of it doth make my faint heart bleed,

And fear doth teach it divination 482 ;

I prophesy thy death, my living sorrow,

If thou encounter with the boar tomorrow.

’But if thou needs wilt 483 hunt, be ruled by me,

Uncouple 484 at the timorous flying 485 hare,

Or at the fox which lives by subtlety 486 ,

Or at the roe 487 which no encounter 488 dare:

Pursue these fearful creatures o’er the downs,

And on thy well-breathed 489 horse keep with thy hounds.

’And when thou hast on foot 490 the purblind 491 hare,

Mark the poor wretch: to overshoot 492 his troubles,

How he outruns the wind and with what care

He cranks and crosses 493 with a thousand doubles 494 .

The many musets 495 through the which he goes

Are like a labyrinth to amaze 496 his foes.

’Sometime he runs among a flock of sheep

To make the cunning 497 hounds mistake their smell,

And sometime where earth-delving conies 498 keep 499

To stop the loud pursuers in their yell,

And sometime sorteth 500 with a herd of deer —

Danger deviseth shifts 501 , wit waits on 502 fear —

’For there his smell with others being mingled,

The hot scent-snuffing hounds are driven to doubt,

Ceasing their clamorous cry till they have singled

With much ado 503 the cold fault 504 cleanly out.

Then do they spend their mouths 505 : echo 506 replies,

As if another chase were in the skies.

’By this, poor Wat 507 , far off upon a hill,

Stands on his hinder legs with list’ning ear

To harken if his foes pursue him still.

Anon their loud alarums 508 he doth hear,

And now his grief may be comparèd well

To one sore 509 sick that hears the passing-bell 510 .

’Then shalt thou see the dew-bedabbled 511 wretch

Turn and return 512 , indenting 513 with the way.

Each envious 514 briar his weary legs do scratch,

Each shadow makes him stop, each murmur stay:

For misery is trodden on by many

And, being low, never relieved 515 by any.

’Lie quietly and hear a little more —

Nay, do not struggle, for thou shalt not rise —

To make thee hate the hunting of the boar:

Unlike myself 516 thou hear’st me moralize 517 ,

Applying this to that and so to so —

For love can comment upon every woe.

‘Where did I leave 518 ?’ ‘No matter where’, quoth he,

’Leave me and then the story aptly 519 ends:

The night is spent.’ ‘Why, what of that?’ quoth she.

‘I am’, quoth he, ’expected of 520 my friends,

And now ‘tis dark and going I shall fall.’

‘In night’, quoth she, ’desire sees best of all.

’But if thou fall, O then imagine this,

The earth, in love with thee, thy footing trips,

And all is but to rob thee of a kiss.

Rich preys make true 521 men thieves: so do thy lips

Make modest Dian 522 cloudy and forlorn,

Lest she should steal a kiss and die forsworn 523 .

’Now of this dark night I perceive the reason 524 :

Cynthia 525 for shame obscures her silver shine,

Till forging 526 nature be condemned of treason

For stealing moulds from heaven that were divine,

Wherein she framed 527 thee, in high heaven’s despite 528 ,

To shame the sun by day and her by night.

’And therefore hath she bribed the destinies 529

To cross 530 the curious 531 workmanship of nature,

To mingle beauty with infirmities

And pure perfection with impure defeature 532 ,

Making it subject to the tyranny

Of mad mischances 533 and much misery,

’As burning fevers, agues 534 pale and faint,

Life-poisoning pestilence 535 and frenzies wood 536 ,

The marrow-eating sickness 537 whose attaint 538

Disorder 539 breeds by heating of the blood 540 ,

Surfeits 541 , impostumes 542 , grief and damned despair:

Swear nature’s death for framing thee so fair.

’And not the least of all these maladies

But in one minute’s fight brings beauty under:

Both favour 543 , savour 544 , hue 545 and qualities,

Whereat th’impartial gazer late did wonder,

Are on the sudden wasted, thawed and done,

As mountain snow melts with the midday sun.

’Therefore, despite of fruitless 546 chastity,

Love-lacking vestals 547 and self-loving 548 nuns

That on the earth would breed a scarcity

And barren dearth 549 of daughters and of sons,

Be prodigal 550 : the lamp that burns by night

Dries up his oil 551 to lend the world his light.

’What is thy body but a swallowing grave,

Seeming to bury that posterity

Which by the rights of time thou needs must have,

If thou destroy them not in dark obscurity 552 ?

If so, the world will hold thee in disdain,

Sith 553 in thy pride 554 so fair a hope is slain 555 .

’So in thyself thyself art made away 556 ,

A mischief 557 worse than civil home-bred strife,

Or theirs whose desperate 558 hands themselves do slay 559 ,

Or butcher-sire 560 that reaves 561 his son of life:

Foul-cank’ring 562 rust the hidden treasure 563 frets 564 ,

But gold that’s put to use 565 more gold begets.’

‘Nay, then,’ quoth Adon, ’you will fall 566 again

Into your idle over-handled 567 theme;

The kiss I gave you is bestowed in vain,

And all in vain you strive against the stream,

For, by this black-faced night, desire’s foul 568 nurse,

Your treatise 569 makes me like you worse and worse.

’If love have lent you twenty thousand tongues,

And every tongue more moving 570 than your own,

Bewitching like the wanton 571 mermaids’ songs,

Yet from mine ear the tempting tune is blown:

For know, my heart stands armèd in mine ear

And will not let a false sound enter there,

’Lest 572 the deceiving harmony should run

Into the quiet closure 573 of my breast,

And then my little heart were quite undone 574 ,

In his bedchamber to be barred of rest:

No, lady, no! My heart longs not to groan,

But soundly sleeps, while now it sleeps alone.

’What have you urged that I cannot reprove 575 ?

The path is smooth that leadeth on to danger:

I hate not love, but your device 576 in love

That lends embracements unto every stranger —

You do it for increase? O strange excuse,

When reason is the bawd 577 to lust’s abuse!

’Call it not love, for love to heaven is fled,

Since sweating lust on earth usurped his name,

Under whose simple semblance 578 he hath fed

Upon fresh beauty, blotting 579 it with blame,

Which 580 the hot tyrant 581 stains 582 and soon bereaves 583 ,

As caterpillars do the tender leaves.

’Love comforteth like sunshine after rain,

But lust’s effect is tempest after sun:

Love’s gentle spring doth always fresh remain,

Lust’s winter comes ere summer half be done:

Love surfeits not, lust like a glutton dies:

Love is all truth, lust full of forgèd lies.

’More I could tell, but more I dare not say:

The text 584 is old, the orator too green 585 .

Therefore in sadness now I will away.

My face is full of shame, my heart of teen 586 ,

Mine ears, that to your wanton talk attended,

Do burn themselves for having so offended.’

With this, he breaketh from the sweet embrace

Of those fair arms which bound him to her breast

And homeward through the dark laund 587 runs apace 588 ,

Leaves love upon her back deeply distressed.

Look how a bright star shooteth from the sky,

So glides he in the night from Venus’ eye 589 ,

Which after him she darts, as one on shore

Gazing upon a late-embarkèd 590 friend 591

Till the wild waves will have him seen no more,

Whose ridges 592 with the meeting clouds contend 593 ;

So did the merciless and pitchy 594 night

Fold in 595 the object that did feed her sight.

Or stonished 597 as night-wand’rers often are,

Having lost the fair discovery of her way 599 .

And now she beats her heart, whereat it groans,

That all the neighbour 600 caves, as seeming troubled,

Make verbal repetition of 601 her moans:

Passion 602 on passion deeply 603 is redoubled,

‘Ay me!’ she cries and twenty times, ‘Woe, woe!’

And twenty echoes twenty times cry so.

She, marking them, begins a wailing note

And sings extemporally 604 a woeful ditty:

How love makes young men thrall 605 and old men dote 606 ,

How love is wise in folly, foolish-witty.

Her heavy 607 anthem 608 still concludes in woe,

And still the choir of echoes answer so.

Her song was tedious and outwore the night,

For lovers’ hours are long, though seeming short:

If pleased themselves, others, they think, delight

In suchlike circumstance, with suchlike sport:

Their copious 609 stories, oftentimes begun,

End without audience and are never done.

For who hath she to spend the night withal 610

Like shrill-tongued tapsters 612 answering every call,

Soothing the humour 613 of fantastic wits 614 ?

She says ‘’Tis so’. They answer all ‘’Tis so’,

And would say after her, if she said ‘No’.

From his moist cabinet 615 mounts up on high

The sun ariseth in his majesty,

Who doth the world so gloriously behold

That cedar tops and hills seem burnished gold.

From whom each lamp and shining star doth borrow

The beauteous influence 617 that makes him bright,

May 619 lend thee light, as thou dost lend to other.’

And all in haste she coasteth 623 to the cry 624 .

Some twined about her thigh to make her stay.

Like a milch doe 625 , whose swelling dugs 626 do ache,

Hasting to feed her fawn hid in some brake 627 .

Where fearfully the dogs exclaim aloud:

They basely fly 638 and dare not stay the field 639 .

Gainst venomed sores the only sovereign plaster 652 .

What may a heavy 674 groan advantage thee?

In likely thoughts the other kills thee quickly.

‘O Jove 713 ,’ quoth she, ’how much a fool was I

To be of such a weak and silly mind

Till mutual overthrow of mortal kind 714 !

For he being dead, with him is beauty slain,

And, beauty dead, black chaos comes again.

’Fie, fie, fond 715 love, thou art as full of fear

As one with treasure laden, hemmed with 716 thieves:

Trifles, unwitnessèd with eye or ear,

Thy coward heart with false bethinking 717 grieves.’

Whereat she leaps that was but late 718 forlorn.

As falcons to the lure 719 , away she flies,

The grass stoops not she treads on it so light,

And in her haste unfortunately 720 spies

Which seen, her eyes, are murdered with the view,

Like stars ashamed of day, themselves withdrew,

Or as the snail, whose tender horns being hit,

Shrinks backward in his shelly cave with pain,

And there, all smothered up, in shade doth sit,

Long after fearing to creep forth again:

So at his bloody view her eyes are fled

Into the deep dark cabins 721 of her head,

Where they resign their office 722 and their light

To the disposing 723 of her troubled brain,

Who bids them still 724 consort with ugly night

And never wound the heart with looks again,

Who 725 , like a king perplexèd 726 in his throne,

By their suggestion 727 gives a deadly groan,

Whereat each tributary subject 728 quakes,

As when the wind, imprisoned in the ground 729 ,

Struggling for passage, earth’s foundation shakes,

Which with cold terror doth men’s minds confound 730 .

This mutiny each part doth so surprise 731

That from their dark beds once more leap her eyes,

And, being opened, threw unwilling light 732

No flower was nigh, no grass, herb, leaf or weed,

But stole 737 his blood and seemed with him to bleed.

Over one shoulder doth she hang her head,

Dumbly she passions 738 , franticly she doteth 739 ,

Her voice is stopped, her joints forget to bow 740 ,

Her eyes are mad that they have wept till now 741 .

That her sight dazzling 742 makes the wound seem three,

And then she reprehends her mangling 743 eye

That makes more gashes where no breach should be:

His face seems twain, each several 744 limb is doubled,

For oft the eye mistakes, the brain being troubled.

’My tongue cannot express my grief for one,

And yet’, quoth she, ’behold two Adons dead!

My sighs are blown away, my salt tears gone,

Mine eyes are turned to fire, my heart to lead.

Heavy heart’s lead melt at mine eyes’ red fire!

So shall I die by drops of hot desire.

’Alas, poor world, what treasure hast thou lost!

What face remains alive that’s worth the viewing?

Whose tongue is music now? What canst thou boast

Of things long since, or any thing ensuing?

The flowers are sweet, their colours fresh and trim 745 ,

But true sweet beauty lived and died with him.

Nor sun nor 747 wind will ever strive to kiss you,

Having no fair 748 to lose, you need not fear:

The sun doth scorn you and the wind doth hiss you,

But when Adonis lived, sun and sharp air

Lurked like two thieves to rob him of his fair,

’And therefore would he put his bonnet on,

Under whose brim the gaudy 749 sun would peep.

The wind would blow it off and, being gone,

And straight 750 , in pity of his tender years,

’To see his face the lion walked along

Behind some hedge because he would not fear 751 him.

The tiger would be tame and gently hear him.

If he had spoke, the wolf would leave his prey

And never fright the silly 753 lamb that day.

He fed them with his sight, they him with berries.

Witness the entertainment that he gave.

He ran upon the boar with his sharp spear,

Who did not whet 761 his teeth at him again 762

And, nuzzling 764 in his 765 flank, the loving swine

With this, she falleth in the place she stood

And stains her face with his congealèd blood.

She looks upon his lips and they are pale,

She takes him by the hand and that is cold

As if they heard the woeful words she told.

Where, lo, two lamps burnt out in darkness lies:

Two glasses 768 , where herself herself beheld

A thousand times and now no more reflect,

Their virtue 769 lost wherein they late excelled,

And every beauty robbed of his 770 effect.

‘Wonder of time,’ quoth she, ’this is my spite,

That, thou being dead, the day should yet be light.

’Since thou art dead, lo, here I prophesy

Sorrow on love hereafter shall attend:

It shall be waited on with jealousy,

Find sweet beginning but unsavoury end,

Ne’er settled equally, but high or low 771 ,

That all love’s pleasure shall not match his woe.

’It shall be fickle, false and full of fraud,

Bud and be blasted 772 in a breathing while 773 ,

The bottom poison and the top o’erstrawed 774

Strike the wise dumb and teach the fool to speak.

’It shall be sparing 777 and too full of riot 778 ,

Teaching decrepit age to tread the measures 779 .

The staring 780 ruffian shall it keep in quiet,

Pluck down the rich, enrich the poor with treasures.

It shall be raging mad and silly 781 mild,

Make the young old, the old become a child.

’It shall suspect where is 782 no cause of fear,

It shall not fear where it should most mistrust,

It shall be merciful and too severe,

And most deceiving when it seems most just 783 ,

Perverse 784 it shall be where it shows most toward 785 ,

Which in round drops upon their whiteness tood.

Since he himself is reft 792 from her by death.

She crops the stalk and in the breach 793 appears

‘Poor flower,’ quoth she, ’this was thy father’s guise 794 ,

Sweet issue 795 of a more sweet-smelling sire,

For every little grief to wet his eyes.

To grow unto himself 796 was his desire,

And so ’tis thine: but know, it is as good

To wither in my breast as in his blood.

’Here was thy father’s bed, here in my breast,

Thou art the next of blood 797 , and ’tis thy right.

Lo, in this hollow cradle 798 take thy rest,

My throbbing heart shall rock thee day and night.

There shall not be one minute in an hour

Wherein I will not kiss my sweet love’s flower.’

Thus weary of the world, away she hies 799

And yokes her silver doves, by whose swift aid

Their mistress, mounted, through the empty skies

In her light chariot quickly is conveyed,

Holding their course to Paphos 800 , where their queen

Means to immure 801 herself and not be seen.
