The Rape of Lucrece

TO THE RIGHT HONOURABLE HENRY WRIOTHESLEY, Earl of Southampton and Baron of Titchfield

The love

1 I dedicate to your lordship is without end: whereof this pamphlet 2 without beginning 3 is but a superfluous moiety 4 . The warrant 5 I have of your honourable disposition, not the worth of my untutored lines, makes it assured of acceptance. What I have done 6 is yours, what I have to do 7 is yours, being 8 part in all I have, devoted yours. Were my worth greater 9 , my duty would show greater: meantime, as it is, it is bound to your lordship, to whom I wish long life still lengthened with all happiness.

Your lordship’s in all duty,

William Shakespeare

THE ARGUMENT 10

Lucius Tarquinius 11 (for his excessive pride surnamed Superbus 12 ) after he had caused his own father-in-law, Servius Tullius 13 , to be cruelly murdered and, contrary to the Roman laws and customs, not requiring or staying 14 for the people’s suffrages 15 , had possessed himself of the kingdom, went, accompanied with his sons and other noblemen of Rome, to besiege Ardea 16 . During which siege the principal men of the army meeting one evening at the tent of Sextus Tarquinius, the king’s son, in their discourses after supper every one commended the virtues of his own wife: among whom Collatinus extolled the incomparable chastity of his wife Lucretia. In that pleasant humour 17 they posted 18 to Rome, and intending by their secret and sudden arrival to make trial of 19 that which every one had before avouched 20 , only Collatinus finds his wife, though it were late in the night, spinning amongst her maids: the other ladies were all found dancing and revelling or in several disports 21 , whereupon the noblemen yielded Collatinus the victory and his wife the fame. At that time Sextus Tarquinius, being inflamed with Lucrece’ beauty yet smothering his passions for the present, departed with the rest back to the camp, from whence he shortly after privily 22 withdrew himself and was, according to his estate 23 , royally entertained and lodged by Lucrece at Collatium 24 . The same night he treacherously stealeth into her chamber, violently ravished 25 her and early in the morning speedeth away. Lucrece, in this lamentable plight, hastily dispatcheth messengers, one to Rome for her father, another to the camp for Collatine. They came, the one accompanied with Junius Brutus, the other with Publius Valerius, and, finding Lucrece attired in mourning habit 26 , demanded the cause of her sorrow. She, first taking an oath of them for her revenge, revealed the actor 27 and whole manner of his dealing 28 and withal 29 suddenly stabbed herself. Which done, with one consent they all vowed to root out the whole hated family of the Tarquins, and bearing the dead body to Rome, Brutus acquainted the people with the doer and manner of the vile deed with a bitter invective against the tyranny of the king: wherewith 30 the people were so moved that, with one consent and a general acclamation, the Tarquins were all exiled and the state government changed from kings to consuls.

From the besiegèd Ardea all in post 31 ,

Borne by the trustless 32 wings of false 33 desire,

Lust-breathèd 34 Tarquin leaves the Roman host 35

And to Collatium bears the lightless 36 fire

Which, in pale embers hid, lurks to aspire 37

And girdle 38 with embracing flames the waist

Of Collatine’s fair love, Lucrece the chaste.

Haply 39 that name of ‘chaste’ unhapp’ly 40 set

This bateless 41 edge on his keen 42 appetite,

When Collatine unwisely did not let 43

To praise the clear unmatchèd red and white 44 ,

Which triumphed in that sky of his delight 45 ,

Where mortal stars 46 , as bright as heaven’s beauties,

With pure aspects 47 did him peculiar duties 48 .

For he the night before in Tarquin’s tent

Unlocked the treasure of his happy state:

What priceless wealth the heavens had him lent

In the possession of his beauteous mate,

Reck’ning his fortune at such high proud rate 49

That kings might be espousèd 50 to more fame,

But 51 king nor peer to such a peerless 52 dame.

O, happiness enjoyed but of 53 a few

And, if possessed, as soon decayed and done 54

As is the morning’s silver-melting dew

Against 55 the golden splendour of the sun,

An expired date, cancelled ere well begun 56 .

Honour and beauty in the owner’s arms

Are weakly fortressed 57 from a world of 58 harms.

Beauty itself doth of itself persuade

The eyes of men without an orator:

What needeth then apology 59 be made

To set forth that which is so singular 60 ?

Or why is Collatine the publisher 61

Of that rich jewel he should keep unknown

From thievish ears because it is his own?

Perchance 62 his boast of Lucrece’ sov’reignty 63

Suggested 64 this proud 65 issue 66 of a king,

For by our ears our hearts oft tainted be.

Perchance that envy of so rich a thing,

Braving compare 67 , disdainfully did sting

His high-pitched 68 thoughts that meaner 69 men should vaunt 70

That golden hap 71 which their superiors want 72 .

But some untimely 73 thought did instigate

His all-too-timeless 74 speed, if none of those:

His honour, his affairs, his friends, his state 75 ,

Neglected all, with swift intent 76 he goes

To quench the coal which in his liver 77 glows.

O rash false heat 78 , wrapped in repentant cold,

Thy hasty spring still blasts 79 and ne’er grows old!

When at Collatium this false lord arrived,

Well was he welcomed by the Roman dame,

Within whose face Beauty and Virtue strived

Which of them both should underprop 80 her fame.

When Virtue bragged, Beauty would blush for shame,

When Beauty boasted blushes, in despite 81

Virtue would stain that o’er with silver white.

But Beauty, in that white intitulèd 82 ,

From Venus’ doves 83 doth challenge that fair 84 field 85 .

Then Virtue claims from Beauty Beauty’s red 86 ,

Which Virtue gave the golden age 87 to gild 88

Their silver cheeks and called it then their shield 89 ,

Teaching them thus to use it in the fight:

When shame assailed 90 , the red should fence 91 the white.

This heraldry 92 in Lucrece’ face was seen,

Argued 93 by Beauty’s red and Virtue’s white:

Of either’s colour was the other queen,

Proving from world’s minority 94 their right.

Yet their ambition makes them still 95 to fight,

The sov’reignty of either being so great

That oft they interchange each other’s seat 96 .

This silent war of lilies and of roses,

Which Tarquin viewed in her fair face’s field,

In their pure ranks his traitor eye encloses 97 ,

Where, lest between them both it should be killed,

The coward captive vanquishèd doth yield

To those two armies that would let him go

Rather than triumph in 98 so false a foe.

Now thinks he that her husband’s shallow tongue,

The niggard prodigal 99 that praised her so,

In that high task hath done her beauty wrong,

Which far exceeds his barren skill to show.

Therefore that praise, which Collatine doth owe 100 ,

Enchanted Tarquin answers 101 with surmise 102 ,

In silent wonder of still-gazing 103 eyes.

This earthly saint, adorèd by this devil,

Little suspecteth the false worshipper:

For unstained thoughts do seldom dream on evil.

Birds never limed 104 no secret 105 bushes fear:

So guiltless she securely 106 gives good cheer 107

And reverend 108 welcome to her princely guest,

Whose inward ill no outward harm expressed.

For that 109 he coloured 110 with his high estate,

Hiding base sin in pleats 111 of majesty,

That 112 nothing in him seemed inordinate 113 ,

Save sometime too much wonder of his eye,

Which, having all, all could not satisfy;

But, poorly rich, so wanteth in his store 114

That, cloyed with much, he pineth 115 still for more.

But she that never coped 116 with stranger 117 eyes

Could pick no meaning from their parling 118 looks,

Nor read the subtle shining secrecies 119

Writ in the glassy margents 120 of such books:

She touched no 121 unknown baits, nor feared no hooks,

Nor could she moralize 122 his wanton sight 123

More than his eyes were opened to the light 124 .

He stories 125 to her ears her husband’s fame,

Won in the fields of fruitful Italy,

And decks 126 with praises Collatine’s high name,

Made glorious by his manly chivalry

With bruisèd arms 127 and wreaths of victory.

Her joy with heaved-up 128 hand she doth express,

And wordless so greets 129 heaven for his success.

Far from the purpose of his coming thither,

He makes excuses for his being there.

No cloudy show of stormy blust’ring weather

Doth yet in his fair 130 welkin 131 once appear

Till sable 132 night, mother of dread and fear,

Upon the world dim darkness doth display

And in her vaulty 133 prison stows 134 the day.

For then is Tarquin brought unto his bed,

Intending 135 weariness with heavy sprite 136 ,

For after supper long he questionèd 137

With modest Lucrece and wore out 138 the night.

Now leaden slumber with life’s strength doth fight

And everyone to rest themselves betake 139 ,

Save thieves and cares 140 and troubled minds that wake.

As one of which doth Tarquin lie revolving 141

The sundry 142 dangers of his will’s obtaining 143 ,

Yet ever to obtain his will resolving,

Though weak-built hopes 144 persuade him to abstaining:

Despair to gain doth traffic oft for gaining 145

And when great treasure 146 is the meed 147 proposed,

Though death be adjunct 148 , there’s no death supposed 149 .

Those that much covet are with gain so fond 150

That what they have not, that which they possess

They scatter and unloose it from their bond 151 ,

And so by hoping 152 more they have but less,

Or gaining more, the profit of excess

Is but to surfeit 153 , and such griefs sustain 154

That they prove bankrupt in this poor rich gain.

The aim of all is but to nurse the life

With honour, wealth and ease in waning age,

And in this aim there is such thwarting strife,

That one for all or all for one we gage 155 :

As 156 life for honour in fell 157 battle’s rage,

Honour for wealth and oft that wealth doth cost

The death of all and all together lost.

So that in vent’ring ill 158 , we leave 159 to be

The things we are for that which we expect 160 :

And this ambitious foul infirmity 161 ,

In having much, torments us with defect 162

Of that we have: so then we do neglect

The thing we have and, all for want of wit 163 ,

Make something nothing by augmenting 164 it.

Such hazard 165 now must doting 166 Tarquin make,

Pawning his honour to obtain his lust,

And for himself himself he must forsake 167 .

Then where is truth, if there be no self-trust?

When shall he think to find a stranger just,

When he himself himself confounds 168 , betrays

To sland’rous tongues and wretched hateful days?

Now stole upon the time the dead of night,

When heavy sleep had closed up mortal eyes.

No comfortable 169 star did lend his light,

No noise but owls’ and wolves’ death-boding cries 170 .

Now serves the season 171 that they may surprise 172

The silly 173 lambs: pure thoughts are dead and still,

While lust and murder wakes to stain and kill.

And now this lustful lord leapt from his bed,

Throwing his mantle 174 rudely 175 o’er his arm,

Is madly tossed between desire and dread:

Th’one sweetly flatters, th’other feareth harm,

But honest fear, bewitched with lust’s foul charm,

Doth too too oft betake him to retire 176 ,

Beaten away by brainsick rude 177 desire.

His falchion 178 on a flint he softly smiteth 179 ,

That from the cold stone sparks of fire do fly,

Whereat a waxen torch forthwith he lighteth,

Which must be lodestar 180 to his lustful eye,

And to the flame thus speaks advisedly 181 ,

’As from this cold flint I enforced this fire,

So Lucrece must I force to my desire.’

Here pale with fear he doth premeditate 182

The dangers of his loathsome enterprise,

And in his inward mind he doth debate

What following sorrow may on this arise.

Then looking scornfully, he doth despise

His naked armour of still-slaughtered lust 183

And justly 184 thus controls his thoughts unjust:

’Fair torch, burn out thy light and lend it not

To darken her whose light excelleth thine,

And die, unhallowed 185 thoughts, before you blot

With your uncleanness that which is divine.

Offer pure incense to so pure a shrine.

Let fair humanity abhor the deed

That spots and stains love’s modest snow-white weed 186 .

’O shame to knighthood and to shining arms!

O foul dishonour to my household’s grave 187 !

O impious act, including all foul harms!

A martial man to be soft 188 fancy’s 189 slave!

True valour still 190 a true respect should have,

Then my digression 191 is so vile, so base,

That it will live engraven in my face.

’Yea, though I die, the scandal will survive

And be an eyesore in my golden coat 192 :

Some loathsome dash 193 the herald will contrive

To cipher me 194 how fondly 195 I did dote 196 ,

That my posterity, shamed with the note 197 ,

Shall curse my bones and hold it for 198 no sin

To wish that I their father had not been.

’What win I, if I gain the thing I seek?

A dream, a breath, a froth 199 of fleeting joy.

Who buys a minute’s mirth to wail a week?

Or sells eternity to get a toy 200 ?

For one sweet grape who will the vine destroy?

Or what fond beggar, but to touch the crown,

Would with the sceptre straight 201 be strucken down?

’If Collatinus dream of my intent,

Will he not wake and in a desp’rate rage

Post 202 hither, this vile purpose to prevent?

This siege that hath engirt 203 his marriage,

This blur 204 to youth, this sorrow to the sage,

This dying virtue, this surviving shame,

Whose crime will bear an ever-during 205 blame?

’O, what excuse can my invention 206 make,

When thou shalt charge me with so black a deed?

Will not my tongue be mute, my frail joints shake,

Mine eyes forgo their light 207 , my false heart bleed?

The guilt being great, the fear doth still exceed 208 ,

And extreme fear can neither fight nor fly 209 ,

But coward-like with trembling terror die.

’Had Collatinus killed my son or sire 210 ,

Or lain in ambush to betray my life,

Or were he not my dear friend, this desire

Might have excuse to work upon 211 his wife,

As in revenge or quittal 212 of such strife.

But as he is my kinsman, my dear friend,

The shame and fault finds no excuse nor end.

’Shameful it is: ay, if the fact 213 be known,

Hateful it is: there is no hate in loving.

I’ll beg her love, but she is not her own 214 :

The worst is but denial and reproving.

My will 215 is strong, past reason’s weak removing 216 :

Who 217 fears a sentence 218 or an old man’s saw 219

Shall by a painted cloth 220 be kept in awe.’

Thus, graceless 221 , holds he disputation 222

’Tween frozen conscience and hot burning will,

And with good thoughts make dispensation 223 ,

Urging the worser sense for vantage still 224 ,

Which in a moment doth confound 225 and kill

All pure effects 226 , and doth so far proceed

That what is vile shows like a virtuous deed.

Quoth 227 he, ’She took me kindly by the hand

And gazed for tidings in my eager eyes,

Fearing some hard 228 news from the warlike band,

Where her belovèd Collatinus lies.

O, how her fear did make her colour rise!

First red as roses that on lawn 229 we lay,

Then white as lawn, the roses took 230 away.

’And how her hand, in my hand being locked,

Forced it 231 to tremble with her loyal fear!

Which 232 struck her sad and then it 233 faster rocked,

Until her husband’s welfare she did hear,

Whereat she smilèd with so sweet a cheer

That, had Narcissus 234 seen her as she stood,

Self-love had never drowned him in the flood.

’Why hunt I then for colour 235 or excuses?

All orators are dumb when beauty pleadeth,

Poor wretches have remorse in poor abuses 236 ,

Love thrives not in the heart that shadows dreadeth:

Affection 237 is my captain and he leadeth,

And when his gaudy 238 banner is displayed,

The coward 239 fights and will not be dismayed 240 .

’Then, childish fear, avaunt 241 ! Debating, die! 242

Respect and reason, wait on wrinkled age!

My heart shall never countermand 243 mine eye;

Sad 244 pause and deep regard beseems 245 the sage:

My part is youth and beats these from the stage.

Desire my pilot is, beauty my prize:

Then who fears sinking 246 where such treasure lies?’

As corn o’ergrown by weeds, so heedful 247 fear

Is almost choked by unresisted 248 lust:

Away he steals with open list’ning ear,

Full of foul hope and full of fond mistrust 249 ,

Both which, as servitors 250 to the unjust,

So cross 251 him with their opposite persuasion 252

That now he vows a league 253 and now invasion.

Within his thought her heavenly image sits,

And in the selfsame seat sits Collatine.

That eye which looks on her confounds his wits 254 ,

That eye which him beholds, as more divine 255 ,

Unto a view 256 so false will not incline 257 ,

But with a pure appeal seeks 258 to the heart,

Which once corrupted takes the worser part,

And therein heartens up 259 his servile powers 260 261 ,

Who, flattered by their leader’s jocund 262 show,

Stuff up 263 his lust, as minutes fill up hours,

And as their captain, so their pride 264 doth grow,

Paying more slavish tribute than they owe.

By reprobate 265 desire thus madly led,

The Roman lord marcheth to Lucrece’ bed.

The locks between her chamber 266 and his will 267 ,

Each one by him enforced, retires his ward 268 ,

But as they open they all rate his ill 269 ,

Which drives the creeping thief to some regard 270 :

The threshold grates the door to have him heard 271 ,

Night-wand’ring weasels 272 shriek to see him there,

They fright him, yet he still pursues his fear.

As each unwilling portal 273 yields him way,

Through little vents and crannies of the place,

The wind wars with his torch to make him stay 274

And blows the smoke of it into his face,

Extinguishing his conduct 275 in this case 276 .

But his hot heart, which fond desire doth scorch,

Puffs forth another wind that fires the torch,

And, being lighted, by the light he spies

Lucretia’s glove, wherein her needle 277 sticks.

He takes it from the rushes 278 where it lies,

And gripping it, the needle his finger pricks,

As who should 279 say, ’This glove to wanton tricks 280

Is not inured 281 . Return again in haste,

Thou see’st our mistress’ ornaments 282 are chaste.’

But all these poor forbiddings could not stay him.

He in the worst sense consters 283 their denial 284 :

The doors, the wind, the glove that did delay him,

He takes for accidental things of trial 285 ,

Or as those bars 286 which stop the hourly dial,

Who with a ling’ring stay his course doth let 287

Till every minute pays the hour his debt.

‘So, so,’ quoth he, ’these lets attend 288 the time,

Like little frosts that sometime threat 289 the spring

To add a more 290 rejoicing to the prime 291

And give the sneapèd 292 birds more cause to sing.

Pain pays the income 293 of each precious thing:

Huge rocks, high winds, strong pirates, shelves 294 and sands,

The merchant fears, ere rich at home he lands.’

Now is he come unto the chamber door

That shuts him from the heaven 295 of his thought 296 ,

Which with a yielding latch and with no more

Hath barred him from the blessèd thing 297 he sought.

So from 298 himself impiety hath wrought 299

That for his prey to pray he doth begin,

As if the heavens should countenance his sin.

But in the midst of his unfruitful prayer,

Having solicited th’eternal power

That his foul 300 thoughts might compass 301 his fair fair,

And they 302 would stand auspicious 303 to the hour,

Even there he starts 304 . Quoth he, ’I must deflow’r.

The powers to whom I pray abhor this fact:

How can they then assist me in the act 305 ?

’Then Love and Fortune be my gods, my guide.

My will is backed with resolution:

Thoughts are but dreams till their effects be tried,

The blackest sin is cleared 306 with absolution 307 ,

Against love’s fire fear’s frost hath dissolution 308 .

The eye of heaven 309 is out 310 , and misty night

Covers the shame that follows sweet delight.’

This said, his guilty hand plucked up the latch,

And with his knee the door he opens wide 311 .

The dove sleeps fast 312 that this night owl will catch.

Thus treason works 313 ere traitors be espied.

Who 314 sees the lurking serpent steps aside,

But she, sound sleeping, fearing no such thing,

Lies at the mercy of his mortal 315 sting 316 .

Into the chamber wickedly he stalks 317

And gazeth on her yet unstainèd bed:

The curtains 318 being close 319 , about he walks,

Rolling his greedy eyeballs in his head.

By their high treason 320 is his heart misled,

Which gives the watchword 321 to his hand full 322 soon

To draw the cloud 323 that hides the silver moon 324 .

Look as 325 the fair and fiery-pointed sun,

Rushing from forth a cloud, bereaves 326 our sight,

Even so, the curtain drawn, his eyes begun

To wink 327 , being blinded with a greater light.

Whether it is that she reflects so bright 328

That dazzleth them, or else some shame supposed,

But blind they are and keep themselves enclosed 329 .

O, had they in that darksome prison 330 died

Then had they seen the period 331 of their ill:

Then Collatine again, by Lucrece’ side,

In his clear 332 bed might have reposèd still.

But they must ope 333 this blessèd league 334 to kill,

And holy-thoughted Lucrece to their sight

Must sell her joy, her life, her world’s delight.

Her lily hand her rosy cheek lies under,

Coz’ning 335 the pillow of a lawful kiss,

Who, therefore angry, seems to part in sunder 336 ,

Swelling 337 on either side to want his 338 bliss,

Between whose hills her head entombèd is,

Where like a virtuous monument 339 she lies,

To be admired of 340 lewd 341 unhallowed 342 eyes.

Without 343 the bed her other fair hand was

On the green coverlet, whose perfect white

Showed like an April daisy on the grass

With pearly sweat resembling dew of night.

Her eyes, like marigolds, had sheathed their light 344

And, canopied in darkness, sweetly lay

Till they might open to adorn the day.

Her hair like golden threads played with her breath —

O modest wantons, wanton modesty! —

Showing life’s triumph in the map 345 of death

And death’s dim look in life’s mortality 346 .

Each 347 in her sleep themselves so beautify,

As if between them twain 348 there were no strife,

But that life lived in death and death in life.

Her breasts, like ivory globes circled with blue,

A pair of maiden 349 worlds unconquerèd,

Save of their lord no bearing yoke 350 they knew,

And him by oath they truly honourèd.

These worlds in Tarquin new ambition bred,

Who like a foul usurper went about

From this fair throne to heave the owner out.

What could he see but mightily he noted?

What did he note but strongly he desired?

What he beheld, on that he firmly doted,

And in his will 351 his wilful eye he tired 352 .

With more than admiration he admired

Her azure 353 veins, her alabaster 354 skin,

Her coral lips, her snow-white dimpled chin.

As the grim 355 lion fawneth o’er his prey,

Sharp hunger by the conquest satisfied,

So o’er this sleeping soul doth Tarquin stay 356 ,

His rage of lust by gazing qualified 357 ,

Slaked 358 , not suppressed, for standing by her side,

His eye, which late 359 this mutiny restrains,

Unto a greater uproar 360 tempts his veins.

And they, like straggling slaves 361 for pillage 362 fighting,

Obdurate 363 vassals 364 fell 365 exploits effecting 366 ,

In bloody death and ravishment 367 delighting,

Nor children’s tears nor 368 mothers’ groans respecting 369 ,

Swell in their pride 370 , the onset 371 still expecting.

Anon 372 his beating heart, alarum 373 striking,

Gives the hot charge 374 and bids them do their liking 375 .

His drumming heart cheers up 376 his burning eye,

His eye commends 377 the leading to his hand,

His hand, as proud of such a dignity,

Smoking with pride, marched on to make his stand 378

On her bare breast, the heart of all her land,

Whose ranks of blue veins, as his hand did scale 379 ,

Left their round turrets destitute and pale.

They, must’ring 380 to the quiet cabinet 381

Where their dear governess and lady lies,

Do tell her she is dreadfully beset 382

And fright her with confusion of their cries.

She, much amazed, breaks ope her locked-up eyes,

Who, peeping forth this tumult to behold,

Are by his flaming torch dimmed and controlled.

Imagine her as one in dead of night

From forth dull sleep by dreadful fancy waking,

That thinks she hath beheld some ghastly 383 sprite 384 ,

Whose grim aspect 385 sets every joint a-shaking —

What terror ’tis! But she, in worser taking 386 ,

From sleep disturbèd, heedfully 387 doth view

The sight which makes supposèd 388 terror true.

Wrapped and confounded in a thousand fears,

Like to a new-killed bird she trembling lies:

She dares not look, yet winking 389 there appears

Quick-shifting 390 antics 391 , ugly in her eyes:

Such shadows 392 are the weak brain’s forgeries 393 ,

Who, angry that the eyes fly from their lights,

In darkness daunts 394 them with more dreadful sights.

His hand that yet remains upon her breast —

Rude ram 395 to batter such an ivory wall —

May feel her heart (poor citizen 396 ) distressed,

Wounding itself to death 397 , rise up and fall,

Beating her bulk 398 that 399 his hand shakes withal 400 .

This moves in him more rage and lesser pity

To make the breach 401 and enter this sweet city.

First like a trumpet doth his tongue begin

To sound a parley 402 to his heartless 403 foe,

Who o’er the white sheet peers her whiter chin,

The reason of this rash alarm 404 to know,

Which he by dumb demeanour 405 seeks to show.

But she with vehement prayers urgeth 406 still

Under what colour 407 he commits this ill.

Thus he replies, ’The colour in thy face,

That even for anger makes the lily pale

And the red rose blush at her own disgrace,

Shall plead for me and tell my loving tale 408 .

Under that colour am I come to scale

Thy never-conquered fort: the fault is thine,

For those thine eyes betray thee unto mine.

’Thus I forestall thee, if thou mean to chide 409 :

Thy beauty hath ensnared thee to this night,

Where thou with patience must my will 410 abide 411 ,

My will that marks thee for my earth’s delight,

Which 412 I to conquer sought with all my might,

But as reproof and reason beat it dead,

By thy bright beauty was it newly bred 413 .

’I see what crosses 414 my attempt will bring,

I know what thorns the growing rose defends,

I think the honey 415 guarded with a sting:

All this beforehand counsel 416 comprehends.

But will is deaf and hears no heedful friends,

Only he hath 417 an eye to gaze on beauty,

And dotes on what he looks, gainst law or duty.

’I have debated, even in my soul,

What wrong, what shame, what sorrow I shall breed

But nothing can affection’s course control

Or stop the headlong fury of his speed.

I know repentant tears ensue 418 the deed,

Reproach, disdain and deadly enmity,

Yet strive I to embrace mine infamy 419 .’

This said, he shakes 420 aloft his Roman blade 421 ,

Which, like a falcon 422 tow’ring in the skies,

Coucheth 423 the fowl below with his wings’ shade,

Whose 424 crooked beak threats if he mount 425 he dies.

So under his insulting 426 falchion 427 lies

Harmless Lucretia, marking what he tells

With trembling fear, as fowl hear falcons’ bells 428 .

‘Lucrece,’ quoth he, ’this night I must enjoy thee.

If thou deny 429 , then force must work my way,

For in thy bed I purpose to destroy 430 thee.

That done, some worthless slave of thine I’ll slay,

To kill thine honour with thy life’s decay,

And in thy dead arms do I mean to place him,

Swearing I slew him seeing thee embrace him.

’So thy surviving husband shall remain

The scornful mark 431 of every open eye,

Thy kinsmen hang their heads at this disdain,

Thy issue blurred 432 with nameless 433 bastardy;

And thou, the author of their obloquy 434 ,

Shalt have thy trespass 435 cited up in rhymes

And sung by children in succeeding times.

’But if thou yield, I rest 436 thy secret friend 437 :

The fault unknown is as a thought unacted,

A little harm done to a great good end

For lawful policy remains enacted 438 .

The poisonous simple 439 sometime is compacted 440

In a pure compound, being so applied

His venom in effect is purified.

’Then, for thy husband and thy children’s sake,

Tender my suit 441 , bequeath not to their lot

The shame that from them no device 442 can take,

The blemish that will never be forgot,

Worse than a slavish wipe 443 or birth-hour’s blot 444 :

For marks descried 445 in men’s nativity

Are nature’s faults, not their own infamy.’

Here with a cockatrice 446 ’ dead-killing eye

He rouseth up himself 447 and makes a pause,

While she, the picture of pure piety,

Like a white hind 448 under the gripe 449 ’s sharp claws,

Pleads 450 in a wilderness where are no laws

To the rough beast that knows no gentle 451 right

Nor aught 452 obeys but his foul appetite.

But when a black-faced cloud the world doth threat,

In his dim mist th’aspiring mountains hiding,

From earth’s dark womb some gentle gust doth get 453 ,

Which blows these pitchy 454 vapours from their biding 455 ,

Hind’ring their present fall 456 by this dividing,

So his unhallowed 457 haste her words delays,

And moody Pluto 458 winks 459 while Orpheus plays.

Yet, foul night-waking cat, he doth but dally 460 ,

While in his holdfast 461 foot the weak mouse panteth.

Her sad behaviour feeds his vulture folly 462 ,

A swallowing gulf 463 that even in plenty wanteth.

His ear her prayers admits 464 , but his heart granteth

No penetrable 465 entrance to her plaining 466 :

Tears harden 467 lust, though marble wear with raining.

Her pity-pleading eyes are sadly fixed

In the remorseless wrinkles of his face.

Her modest eloquence with sighs is mixed,

Which to her oratory adds more grace.

She puts the period 468 often from his place 469 ,

And midst the sentence so her accent 470 breaks

That twice she doth begin ere once she speaks.

She conjures him by 471 high almighty Jove 472 ,

By knighthood, gentry 473 and sweet friendship’s oath,

By her untimely 474 tears, her husband’s love,

By holy human law and common troth 475 ,

By heaven and earth and all the power of both,

That to his borrowed 476 bed he make retire 477

And stoop 478 to honour, not to foul desire.

Quoth she, ’Reward not hospitality

With such black payment as thou hast pretended 479 ,

Mud 480 not the fountain that gave drink to thee,

Mar 481 not the thing 482 that cannot be amended,

End thy ill aim 483 before thy shoot 484 be ended 485 .

He is no woodman 486 that doth bend his bow

To strike 487 a poor unseasonable 488 doe.

’My husband is thy friend, for his sake spare me.

Thyself art mighty, for thine own sake leave me:

Myself a weakling, do not then ensnare me.

Thou look’st not like deceit, do not deceive me.

My sighs, like whirlwinds, labour hence to heave 489 thee.

If ever man were moved 490 with woman’s moans,

Be movèd with my tears, my sighs, my groans,

’All which together, like a troubled ocean,

Beat at thy rocky and wrack-threat’ning 491 heart,

To soften it with their continual motion,

For stones dissolved to water do convert.

O, if no harder than a stone thou art,

Melt at my tears and be compassionate:

Soft pity enters at an iron gate.

’In Tarquin’s likeness I did entertain thee:

Hast thou put on his shape 492 to do him shame?

To all the host of heaven I complain me.

Thou wrong’st his honour, wound’st his princely name:

Thou art not what thou seem’st, and if the same,

Thou seem’st not what thou art, a god, a king,

For kings like gods should govern everything 493 .

’How will thy shame be seeded 494 in thine age

When thus thy vices bud before thy spring?

If in thy hope 495 thou dar’st do such outrage 496 ,

What dar’st thou not when once thou art a king?

O, be remembered, no outrageous thing

From vassal actors 497 can be wiped away,

Then kings’ misdeeds cannot be hid in clay 498 .

’This deed will make thee only loved for 499 fear,

But happy 500 monarchs still 501 are feared 502 for love.

With foul offenders thou perforce must bear,

When they in thee the like offences prove 503 .

If but for fear of this, thy will remove 504 .

For princes are the glass 505 , the school, the book,

Where subjects’ eyes do learn, do read, do look.

’And wilt thou be the school where lust shall learn?

Must he in thee read lectures 506 of such shame?

Wilt thou be glass wherein it shall discern

Authority for sin, warrant 507 for blame,

To privilege 508 dishonour in thy name?

Thou back’st 509 reproach against long-living laud 510

And mak’st fair reputation but a bawd 511 .

’Hast thou command 512 ? By him that gave it thee,

From a pure heart command thy rebel will:

Draw not thy sword to guard iniquity 513 ,

For it was lent thee all that brood 514 to kill.

Thy princely office 515 how canst thou fulfil,

When, patterned by 516 thy fault, foul sin may say,

He learned to sin and thou didst teach the way?

’Think but how vile a spectacle it were

To view thy present trespass in another.

Men’s faults do seldom to themselves appear:

Their own transgressions partially they smother.

This guilt would seem death-worthy in thy brother.

O, how are they wrapped in with infamies 517

That from their own misdeeds askance 518 their eyes!

’To thee, to thee, my heaved-up 519 hands appeal,

Not to seducing lust, thy rash relier 520 .

I sue for exiled majesty’s repeal 521 :

Let him return and flatt’ring 522 thoughts retire.

His true respect 523 will prison false desire

And wipe the dim mist from thy doting eyne 524 ,

That thou shalt see thy state 525 and pity mine.’

‘Have done’, quoth he. ’My uncontrollèd tide

Turns not, but swells 526 the higher by this let 527 .

Small lights are soon blown out, huge fires abide

And with the wind in greater fury fret:

The petty streams that pay a daily debt

To their salt sovereign 528 with their fresh falls’ haste

Add to his flow, but alter not his taste.’

‘Thou art’, quoth she, ’a sea, a sovereign king,

And, lo, there falls into thy boundless flood

Black lust, dishonour, shame, misgoverning,

Who seek to stain the ocean of thy blood 529 .

If all these petty ills shall change thy good,

Thy sea within a puddle’s womb is hearsed 530 ,

And not the puddle in thy sea dispersed.

’So shall these slaves 531 be king and thou their slave,

Thou nobly base, they basely dignified,

Thou their fair life and they thy fouler grave,

Thou loathèd in their shame, they in thy pride.

The lesser thing should not the greater hide.

The cedar stoops not to the base shrub’s foot,

But low shrubs wither at the cedar’s root.

‘So let thy thoughts, low vassals 532 to thy state —’

‘No more’, quoth he, ’by heaven, I will not hear thee.

Yield to my love. If not, enforcèd hate

Instead of love’s coy 533 touch shall rudely tear thee.

That done, despitefully 534 I mean to bear thee

Unto the base bed of some rascal groom 535

To be thy partner in this shameful doom 536 .’

This said, he sets his foot upon the light,

For light and lust are deadly enemies:

Shame folded up in blind concealing night,

When most unseen, then most doth tyrannize.

The wolf hath seized 537 his prey, the poor lamb cries,

Till with her own white fleece 538 her voice controlled 539

Entombs her outcry in her lips’ sweet fold 540 .

For with the nightly linen 541 that she wears

He pens her piteous clamours in her head,

Cooling his hot face in the chastest tears

That ever modest eyes with sorrow shed.

O, that prone 542 lust should stain so pure a bed,

The spots whereof could weeping 543 purify,

Her tears should drop on them perpetually!

But she hath lost a dearer thing than life

And he hath won what he would lose again:

This forcèd league 544 doth force a further strife,

This momentary joy breeds months of pain,

This hot desire converts to cold disdain,

Pure chastity is rifled 545 of her store,

And lust, the thief, far poorer than before.

Look as the full-fed hound or gorgèd 546 hawk,

Unapt 547 for tender smell or speedy flight,

Make slow pursuit, or altogether balk 548

The prey wherein by nature they delight:

So surfeit-taking 549 Tarquin fares this night:

His taste delicious, in digestion souring,

Devours his will that lived by foul devouring.

O, deeper sin than bottomless conceit 550

Can comprehend in still imagination 551 !

Drunken desire must vomit his receipt 552 ,

Ere he can see his own abomination.

While lust is in his pride 553 , no exclamation 554

Can curb his heat or rein his rash desire,

Till like a jade 555 self-will himself doth tire.

And then with lank and lean discoloured cheek,

With heavy eye, knit brow and strengthless pace,

Feeble desire, all recreant 556 , poor and meek,

Like to a bankrupt beggar wails his case:

The flesh being proud 557 , desire doth fight with grace,

For there 558 it revels 559 and when that decays 560 ,

The guilty rebel for remission 561 prays.

So fares it with this faultful lord of Rome,

Who this accomplishment so hotly chased,

For now against himself he sounds this doom 562 ,

That through the length of times he stands disgraced.

Besides, his soul’s fair temple is defaced,

To whose weak ruins muster troops of cares,

To ask the spotted princess 563 how she fares.

She says her subjects 564 with foul insurrection

Have battered down her consecrated wall,

And by their mortal 565 fault brought in subjection

Her immortality and made her thrall 566

To living death and pain perpetual,

Which in her prescience 567 she controllèd still 568 ,

But 569 her foresight could not forestall their will.

Ev’n in this thought through the dark night he stealeth,

A captive victor that hath lost in gain,

Bearing away the wound that nothing healeth,

The scar that will, despite of cure, remain,

Leaving his spoil 570 perplexed 571 in greater pain.

She bears the load of lust 572 he left behind,

And he the burden of a guilty mind.

He like a thievish dog creeps sadly thence,

She like a wearied lamb lies panting there.

He scowls and hates himself for his offence,

She, desperate 573 , with her nails her flesh doth tear.

He faintly flies 574 , sweating with guilty fear,

She stays, exclaiming on 575 the direful night.

He runs and chides his vanished, loathed delight.

He thence departs a heavy convertite 576 ,

She there remains a hopeless castaway 577 .

He in his speed looks for the morning light,

She prays she never may behold the day.

‘For day’, quoth she, ’Night’s scapes 578 doth open lay 579 ,

And my true eyes have never practised how

To cloak offences with a cunning brow 580 .

’They think not but 581 that every eye can see

The same disgrace which they themselves behold,

And therefore would they still in darkness be 582 ,

To have their unseen sin remain untold.

For they their guilt with weeping will unfold,

And grave 583 , like water that doth eat in steel,

Upon my cheeks what helpless shame I feel.’

Here she exclaims against repose and rest,

And bids her eyes hereafter still be blind.

She wakes her heart by beating on her breast

And bids it leap from thence, where it may find

Some purer chest to close 584 so pure a mind.

Frantic with grief thus breathes she forth her spite 585

Against the unseen secrecy of night:

’O comfort-killing Night! Image of hell!

Dim register 586 and notary 587 of shame!

Black stage 588 for tragedies and murders fell 589 !

Vast sin-concealing chaos 590 ! Nurse of blame 591 !

Blind, muffled bawd! Dark harbour for defame 592 !

Grim cave of death! Whisp’ring conspirator

With close-tongued 593 treason and the ravisher!

’O hateful, vaporous and foggy Night!

Since thou art guilty of my cureless crime,

Muster 594 thy mists to meet the eastern light,

Make war against proportioned course of time 595 ,

Or if thou wilt permit the sun to climb

His wonted 596 height, yet ere he go to bed,

Knit 597 poisonous clouds about his golden head.

’With rotten damps 598 ravish 599 the morning air,

Let their exhaled unwholesome breaths make sick

The life 600 of purity, the supreme fair,

Ere he arrive 601 his weary noontide prick 602 ,

And let thy musty vapours march so thick

That in their smoky ranks his smothered light

May set at noon and make perpetual night.

’Were Tarquin Night, as he is but Night’s child,

The silver-shining queen 603 he would distain 604 .

Her twinkling handmaids 605 too, by him defiled,

Through Night’s black bosom should not peep again.

So should I have co-partners in my pain,

And fellowship in woe doth woe assuage,

As palmers 606 ’ chat makes short their pilgrimage.

’Where now I have no one to blush with me,

To cross their arms 607 and hang their heads with mine,

To mask their brows and hide their infamy,

But I alone alone must sit and pine 608 ,

Seasoning the earth with showers of silver brine,

Mingling my talk with tears, my grief with groans,

Poor wasting monuments of lasting moans 609 .

’O Night, thou furnace of foul-reeking 610 smoke,

Let not the jealous day behold that face

Which underneath thy black all-hiding cloak

Immodestly lies martyred with disgrace.

Keep still possession of thy gloomy place,

That all the faults which in thy reign are made

May likewise be sepulchred 611 in thy shade.

’Make me not object 612 to the tell-tale day:

The light will show, charactered in my brow,

The story of sweet chastity’s decay,

The impious breach of holy wedlock vow.

Yea, the illiterate, that know not how

To cipher 613 what is writ in learnèd books,

Will quote 614 my loathsome trespass 615 in my looks.

’The nurse, to still her child, will tell my story

And fright her crying babe with Tarquin’s name.

The orator, to deck 616 his oratory,

Will couple 617 my reproach to Tarquin’s shame.

Feast-finding minstrels 618 , tuning my defame 619 ,

Will tie 620 the hearers to attend each line,

How Tarquin wrongèd me, I Collatine 621 .

’Let my good name, that senseless reputation 622 ,

For Collatine’s dear love be kept unspotted.

If that be made a theme for disputation 623 ,

The branches of another root 624 are rotted,

And undeserved reproach to him allotted

That is as clear 625 from this attaint 626 of mine

As I, ere this, was pure to Collatine.

’O unseen shame! Invisible disgrace!

O unfelt sore! Crest-wounding 627 , private scar!

Reproach is stamped in Collatinus’ face,

And Tarquin’s eye may read the mot 628 afar,

How he in peace is wounded, not in war.

Alas, how many bear such shameful blows,

Which not themselves, but he that gives them knows.

’If, Collatine, thine honour lay in me,

From me by strong assault it is bereft 629 :

My honey lost and I, a drone-like 630 bee,

Have no perfection 631 of my summer left,

But robbed and ransacked by injurious 632 theft.

In thy weak hive a wand’ring wasp hath crept

And sucked the honey which thy chaste bee kept.

’Yet am I guilty of thy honour’s wrack 633 ,

Yet for thy honour did I entertain him 634 :

Coming from thee, I could not put him back 635 ,

For it had been dishonour to disdain 636 him.

Besides, of weariness he did complain him

And talked of virtue — O unlooked-for evil

When virtue is profaned in such a devil!

’Why should the worm 637 intrude the maiden bud?

Or hateful cuckoos 638 hatch in sparrows’ nests?

Or toads infect fair founts 639 with venom 640 mud?

Or tyrant folly 641 lurk in gentle 642 breasts?

Or kings be breakers of their own behests 643 ?

But no perfection is so absolute

That some impurity doth not pollute.

’The agèd man that coffers up 644 his gold

Is plagued with cramps and gouts and painful fits

And scarce hath eyes his treasure to behold,

But like still-pining 645 Tantalus 646 he sits

And useless barns 647 the harvest of his wits,

Having no other pleasure of his gain

But torment that it cannot cure his pain.

’So then he hath it when he cannot use it

And leaves it to be mastered by his young,

Who in their pride do presently 648 abuse it:

Their father was too weak and they too strong

To hold their cursèd-blessèd fortune long.

The sweets we wish for turn to loathèd sours

Even in the moment that we call them ours.

’Unruly blasts wait on the tender spring,

Unwholesome weeds take root with precious flowers,

The adder hisses where the sweet birds sing,

What virtue breeds iniquity devours.

We have no good that we can say is ours,

But ill-annexèd Opportunity 649

Or kills his life or 650 else his quality 651 .

’O Opportunity, thy guilt is great!

’Tis thou that execut’st 652 the traitor’s treason:

Thou sets 653 the wolf where he the lamb may get.

Whoever plots the sin, thou point’st 654 the season 655 .

’Tis thou that spurn’st 656 at right, at law, at reason,

And in thy shady cell 657 , where none may spy him,

Sits Sin, to seize the souls that wander by him.

’Thou mak’st the vestal 658 violate her oath,

Thou blow’st the fire when temperance 659 is thawed,

Thou smother’st honesty 660 ; thou murd’rest troth 661 :

Thou foul abettor, thou notorious bawd,

Thou plantest scandal and displacest laud 662 .

Thou ravisher, thou traitor, thou false thief,

Thy honey turns to gall 663 , thy joy to grief.

’Thy secret pleasure turns to open shame,

Thy private feasting to a public fast,

Thy smoothing 664 titles to a ragged name 665 ,

Thy sugared tongue to bitter wormwood 666 taste:

Thy violent vanities 667 can never last.

How comes it then, vile Opportunity,

Being so bad, such numbers seek for thee?

’When wilt thou be the humble suppliant’s 668 friend

And bring him where his suit may be obtained?

When wilt thou sort 669 an hour great strifes to end?

Or free that soul which wretchedness hath chained?

Give physic 670 to the sick? Ease to the pained?

The poor, lame, blind, halt 671 , creep, cry out for thee,

But they ne’er meet with Opportunity.

’The patient dies while the physician sleeps,

The orphan pines 672 while the oppressor feeds.

Justice is feasting while the widow weeps,

Advice 673 is sporting 674 while infection breeds.

Thou grant’st no time for charitable deeds.

Wrath, envy, treason, rape and murder’s rages,

Thy heinous hours wait on them as their pages 675 .

’When Truth and Virtue have to do with thee,

A thousand crosses 676 keep them from thy aid.

He gratis 677 comes and thou art well apaid 678

My Collatine would else 679 have come to me

When Tarquin did, but he was stayed 680 by thee.

’Guilty thou art of murder and of theft,

Guilty of perjury and subornation 681 ,

Guilty of treason, forgery and shift 682 ,

Guilty of incest, that abomination:

An accessary by thine inclination 683

To all sins past and all that are to come,

From the creation to the general doom 684 .

’Misshapen Time, copesmate 685 of ugly Night,

Swift subtle post 686 , carrier 687 of grisly care,

Eater of youth, false slave to false delight,

Base watch of woes 688 , sin’s packhorse 689 , virtue’s snare,

Thou nursest all and murd’rest all that are.

O, hear me then, injurious, shifting Time,

Be guilty of my death, since of 690 my crime.

’Why hath thy servant, Opportunity,

Betrayed the hours thou gav’st me to repose?

Cancelled my fortunes and enchainèd me

To endless date 691 of never-ending woes?

Time’s office 692 is to fine 693 the hate of foes,

To eat up errors by opinion 694 bred,

Not spend the dowry of a lawful bed.

’Time’s glory is to calm contending 695 kings,

To unmask falsehood and bring truth to light,

To stamp the seal of time in agèd things,

To wake the morn and sentinel 696 the night,

To wrong the wronger till he render right,

To ruinate 697 proud buildings with thy hours

And smear with dust their glitt’ring golden towers,

’To fill with worm-holes stately monuments,

To feed oblivion with decay of things,

To blot old books and alter their contents,

To pluck the quills 698 from ancient ravens’ wings,

To dry the old oak’s sap and cherish springs 699 ,

To spoil antiquities of hammered steel

And turn the giddy round of Fortune’s wheel 700 ,

’To show the beldame 701 daughters of her daughter,

To make the child a man, the man a child 702 ,

To slay the tiger that doth live by slaughter,

To tame the unicorn and lion wild,

To mock the subtle 703 in themselves beguiled 704 ,

To cheer the ploughman with increaseful 705 crops

And waste 706 huge stones with little water drops.

’Why work’st thou mischief 707 in thy pilgrimage,

Unless thou couldst return to make amends?

One poor retiring 708 minute in an age

Would purchase thee a thousand thousand friends,

Lending him wit that to bad debtors lends 709 .

O, this dread Night, wouldst thou one hour come back,

I could prevent this storm and shun thy wrack.

’Thou ceaseless lackey 710 to eternity,

With some mischance cross 711 Tarquin in his flight,

Devise extremes beyond extremity

To make him curse this cursèd crimeful night,

Let ghastly 712 shadows his lewd 713 eyes affright,

’Disturb his hours of rest with restless trances 714 ,

To make him moan, but pity not his moans.

And let mild women to him lose their mildness,

Wilder to him than tigers in their wildness.

’Let him have time to tear his curlèd hair,

Let him have time against himself to rave,

Let him have time of Time’s help to despair,

Let him have time to live a loathèd slave,

Let him have time a beggar’s orts 716 to crave,

And time to see one that by alms doth live 717

’Let him have time to see his friends his foes

And merry fools to mock at him resort 720 ,

And ever let his unrecalling 722 crime

’O Time, thou tutor both to good and bad,

Teach me to curse him that 724 thou taught’st this ill.

At his own shadow let the thief run mad,

Himself himself seek every hour to kill:

Such wretched hands such wretched blood should spill,

For who so base would such an office have

As sland’rous deathsman 725 to so base a slave?

’The baser is he, coming 726 from a king,

To shame his hope 727 with deeds degenerate.

The mightier man, the mightier is the thing

That makes him honoured or begets him hate 728 :

For greatest scandal waits on 729 greatest state 730 .

The moon being clouded presently 731 is missed,

But little stars may hide them when they list.

’The crow may bathe his coal-black wings in mire 732

But if the like 733 the snow-white swan desire,

The stain upon his silver down will stay.

Poor grooms 734 are sightless night, kings glorious day,

But eagles gazed upon with every eye.

’Out 735 , idle 736 words, servants to shallow fools,

Unprofitable sounds, weak arbitrator 737 s!

Busy yourselves in skill-contending schools 738 ,

For me, I force not argument a straw 739 ,

Since that my case 740 is past the help of law.

’In vain I rail 741 at Opportunity,

At Time, at Tarquin and uncheerful Night,

In vain I cavil 742 with mine infamy,

In vain I spurn 743 at my confirmed despite 744 :

This helpless smoke of words doth me no right.

The remedy indeed to do me good

Is to let forth my foul defilèd blood 745 .

’Poor hand, why quiver’st thou at this decree?

Honour thyself to rid me of this shame,

For if I die my honour lives in thee,

But if I live thou liv’st in my defame.

Since thou couldst not defend thy loyal dame

And wast afeard to scratch her wicked foe,

Kill both thyself and her for yielding so.’

This said, from her betumbled couch 746 she starteth,

To find some desp’rate instrument of death.

‘In vain,’ quoth she, ’I live and seek in vain

I feared by Tarquin’s falchion 753 to be slain,

Yet for the self-same purpose seek a knife;

But when I feared I was a loyal wife.

So am I now — O no, that cannot be!

Of that true type 754 hath Tarquin rifled me.

’O, that is gone for which I sought to live,

Poor helpless help, the treasure stol’n away,

’Well, well, dear Collatine, thou shalt not know

The stainèd taste of violated troth,

To flatter 758 thee with an infringèd 759 oath.

This bastard graff 760 shall never come to growth:

’Nor shall he smile at thee in secret thought,

But thou shalt know thy int’rest 762 was not bought

’I will not poison thee with my attaint 764

By this 769 , lamenting Philomel 770 had ended

The well-tuned warble of her nightly sorrow,

And solemn night with slow, sad gait descended

To ugly hell, when, lo, the blushing morrow

Lends light to all fair eyes that light will borrow.

But cloudy 771 Lucrece shames herself to see,

And therefore still in night would cloistered be.

Revealing day through every cranny spies

To whom she sobbing speaks: ’O eye of eyes,

Why pry’st thou 772 through my window? Leave thy peeping,

Mock with thy tickling beams eyes that are sleeping,

Brand not my forehead with thy piercing light,

For day hath nought to do 773 what’s done by night.’

Thus cavils she with everything she sees:

True grief is fond 774 and testy 775 as a child,

Who wayward 776 once, his mood with nought agrees:

Old woes, not infant sorrows, bear them mild 777 ,

Continuance 778 tames the one, the other wild,

Like an unpractised swimmer plunging still,

With too much labour drowns for want of skill.

So she, deep-drenchèd 779 in a sea of care,

Holds disputation with each thing she views

And to herself all sorrow doth compare.

No object but her passion’s strength renews 780 ,

And as one shifts, another straight 781 ensues.

Sometime her grief is dumb and hath no words,

Sometime ’tis mad and too much talk affords.

The little birds that tune their morning’s joy

Make her moans mad with their sweet melody,

For mirth doth search the bottom of annoy 782 .

Sad souls are slain in merry company,

Grief best is pleased with grief’s society:

True sorrow then is feelingly sufficed 783

When with like semblance it is sympathized 784 .

’Tis double death to drown in ken 785 of shore,

He ten times pines 786 that pines beholding food,

To see the salve 787 doth make the wound ache more,

Great grief grieves most at that 788 would do it good,

Who, being stopped, the bounding 789 banks o’erflows:

Grief dallied 790 with nor law nor 791 limit knows.

‘You mocking-birds,’ quoth she, ’your tunes entomb

My restless 792 discord loves no stops nor rests 793 :

A woeful hostess brooks 794 not merry guests.

’This brief abridgement of my will I make:

My soul and body to 833 the skies and ground,

My resolution, husband, do thou take,

This plot of death when sadly she had laid

For why 844 her face wore sorrow’s livery,

Their gentle sex to weep are often willing,

For men have marble, women waxen minds,

And therefore are they formed as marble will 853 :

The weak oppressed, th’impression of strange kinds 854

Lays open 858 all the little worms that creep.

In men, as in a rough-grown grove, remain

Cave-keeping 859 evils that obscurely 860 sleep.

Through crystal walls each little mote 861 will peep,

Though men can cover crimes with bold stern looks,

Poor women’s faces are their own faults’ books.

No man inveigh against 862 the withered flow’r,

But chide 863 rough winter that the flow’r hath killed:

Not that devoured, but that which doth devour,

Is worthy blame. O, let it not be hild 864

Poor women’s faults, that they are so fulfilled

With men’s abuses: those proud lords, to blame,

Make weak-made women tenants to their 865 shame.

The precedent 866 whereof in Lucrece’ view,

Assailed 867 by night with circumstances strong

Of present death 868 and shame that might ensue

By that 869 her death, to do her husband wrong.

Such danger to resistance did belong

That dying fear 870 through all her body spread,

And who cannot abuse a body dead?

By this, mild patience bid fair Lucrece speak

To the poor counterfeit 871 of her complaining:

‘My girl,’ quoth she, ’on what occasion break

Those tears from thee that down thy cheeks are raining?

If thou dost weep for grief of my sustaining 872 ,

Know, gentle wench, it small avails 873 my mood:

If tears could help, mine own would do me good.

‘But tell me, girl, when went’ — and there she stayed 874

Till after a deep groan — ‘Tarquin from hence?’

‘Madam, ere I was up’, replied the maid,

’The more to blame my sluggard 875 negligence.

Yet with the fault I thus far can dispense 876 :

Myself was stirring ere the break of day,

And, ere I rose, was Tarquin gone away.

’But, lady, if your maid may be so bold,

She would request to know your heaviness 877 .’

‘O, peace!’ quoth Lucrece. ’If it should be told,

The repetition cannot make it less:

For more it is than I can well express,

And that deep torture may be called a hell

When more is felt than one hath power to tell.

’Go, get me hither paper, ink and pen —

Yet save that labour, for I have them here.

What should I say? One of my husband’s men

Bid thou be ready, by and by, to bear

A letter to my lord, my love, my dear.

Bid him with speed prepare to carry it.

The cause craves haste and it will soon be writ.’

Her maid is gone and she prepares to write,

First hovering o’er the paper with her quill.

Conceit 878 and grief an eager combat fight:

What wit 879 sets down is blotted straight 880 with will 881 .

This is too curious good 882 , this blunt and ill 883 :

Much like a press of people at a door

Throng her inventions 884 which shall go before 885 .

At last she thus begins: ’Thou worthy lord

Of that unworthy wife that greeteth thee,

Health to thy person. Next vouchsafe t’afford 886 —

If ever, love, thy Lucrece thou wilt see —

Some present speed to come and visit me.

So I commend me 887 , from our house in grief:

My woes are tedious 888 though my words are brief.’

Here folds she up the tenor 889 of her woe,

Her certain sorrow writ uncertainly.

By this short schedule 890 Collatine may know

Her grief, but not her grief’s true quality:

She dares not thereof make discovery 891 ,

Lest he should hold it her own gross abuse,

Ere she with blood had stained her stained excuse 892 .

Besides, the life and feeling of her passion 893

She hoards, to spend when he is by to hear her,

When sighs and groans and tears may grace the fashion

Of her disgrace, the better so to clear her

From that suspicion which the world might bear her.

To shun this blot, she would not blot the letter

With words, till action 894 might become 895 them better.

To see sad sights moves more than hear them told,

For then the eye interprets to the ear

The heavy motion 896 that it doth behold,

When every part a part of woe doth bear 897 .

’Tis but a part of sorrow that we hear:

Deep sounds 898 make lesser noise than shallow fords,

And sorrow ebbs, being blown with wind of words 899 .

Her letter now is sealed and on it writ

‘At Ardea to my lord with more than haste’.

The post 900 attends and she delivers it,

Charging 901 the sour-faced groom to hie as fast

As lagging 902 fowls before the northern blast.

Speed more than speed but dull and slow she deems 903 :

Extremity still urgeth such extremes.

The homely villain 904 curtsies 905 to her low,

And, blushing on her, with a steadfast eye

Receives the scroll without or yea 906 or no

And forth with bashful innocence doth hie.

But they whose guilt within their bosoms lie

Imagine every eye beholds their blame:

For Lucrece thought he blushed to see her shame,

When, silly 907 groom, God wot 908 , it was defect 909

Of spirit, life and bold audacity.

Such harmless creatures have a true respect

To talk in deeds, while others saucily

Promise more speed, but do it leisurely.

Even so this pattern of the worn-out age 910

Pawned 911 honest looks, but laid no words to gage 912 .

His kindled 913 duty kindled her mistrust,

That two red fires in both their faces blazed.

And yet the duteous vassal scarce is gone.

The weary time she cannot entertain 917 ,

For now ’tis stale 918 to sigh, to weep and groan:

So woe hath wearied woe, moan tirèd moan,

Pausing for means to mourn some newer way.

At last she calls to mind where hangs a piece

Many a dry drop seemed a weeping tear

The red blood reeked 931 , to show the painter’s strife 932 ,

And dying eyes gleamed forth their ashy lights,

There might you see the labouring pioneer 933

Begrimed with sweat and smearèd all with dust,

And from the towers of Troy there would appear

Gazing upon the Greeks with little lust 935 :

Such sweet observance 936 in this work was had

In great commanders grace and majesty

In youth, quick 937 bearing and dexterity.

And here and there the painter interlaces 938

In Ajax and Ulysses 939 , O, what art

Of physiognomy 940 might one behold!

The face of either ciphered 941 either’s 942 heart.

The scalps of many, almost hid behind,

Here one man’s hand leaned on another’s head,

In such relenting 1138 dew of lamentations,

But kneel with me and help to bear thy part

To rouse our Roman gods with invocations 1139 ,

That they will suffer 1140 these abominations —

Since Rome herself in them doth stand disgraced —

By our strong arms from forth her fair streets chased.

’Now, by the Capitol 1141 that we adore,

And by this chaste blood so unjustly stained,

By heaven’s fair sun that breeds the fat 1142 earth’s store,

By all our country rights in Rome maintained,

And by chaste Lucrece’ soul that late complained

Her wrongs to us, and by this bloody knife

We will revenge the death of this true wife.’

This said, he struck his hand upon his breast,

And kissed the fatal knife, to end his vow,

And to his protestation 1143 urged the rest,

Who, wond’ring 1144 at him, did his words allow 1145 .

Then jointly to the ground their knees they bow,

And that deep vow, which Brutus made before,

He doth again repeat and that they swore.

When they had sworn to this advisèd doom 1146 ,

They did conclude to bear dead Lucrece thence,

To show her bleeding body thorough 1147 Rome,

And so to publish Tarquin’s foul offence;

Which being done with speedy diligence,

The Romans plausibly 1148 did give consent

To Tarquin’s everlasting banishment.

To the Queen

1

As the dial 2 hand tells o’er

The same hours it had before,

Still beginning in the ending,

Circular account still lending,

So, most mighty Queen we pray,

Like the dial day by day

You may lead the seasons on,

Making new when old are gone,

That the babe which now is young

And hath yet no use of tongue

Many a Shrovetide 3 here may bow

To that empress I do now,

That the children of these lords,

Sitting at your council boards 4 ,

May be grave and agèd seen

Of her that was their fathers’ queen.

Once I wish this wish again,

Heaven subscribe it with ‘Amen’.

NOTES

1 To the Queen

2 dial clock

3 Shrovetide the three days immediately preceding the season of Lent; this epilogue was written for a court performance by Shakespeare’s company on Shrove Tuesday, 20 February 1599

4 boards tables

‘Let the Bird of Loudest Lay’

1

LET the bird of loudest lay 2 3

On the sole Arabian tree

Herald sad and trumpet 4 be,

To whose sound chaste wings obey.

But thou shrieking harbinger 5 ,

Foul precurrer 6 of the fiend 7 ,

Augur 8 of the fever’s end,

To this troop come thou not near.

From this session 9 interdict 10

Every fowl of tyrant wing 11 ,

Save 12 the eagle, feathered king:

Keep the obsequy 13 so strict 14 .

Let the priest in surplice 15 white,

That defunctive 16 music can 17 ,

Be the death-divining 18 swan,

Lest the requiem lack his right 19 .

And thou treble-dated 20 crow,

That thy sable gender 21 mak’st

With the breath thou giv’st and tak’st,

’Mongst our mourners shalt thou go.

Here the anthem 22 doth commence:

Love and constancy is dead;

Phoenix 23 and the turtle 24 fled 25

In a mutual flame 26 from hence.

So they loved, as 27 love in twain 28

Had the essence but in one 29 ,

Two distincts 30 , division none:

Number 31 there in love was slain.

Hearts remote 32 , yet not asunder 33 ;

Distance 34 and no space was seen

’Twixt this turtle and his queen:

But in them it were a wonder 35 .

So between them love did shine

That the turtle saw his right 36

Flaming in the phoenix’ sight 37 :

Either was the other’s mine 38 .

Property 39 was thus appalled

That the self was not the same 40 :

Single nature’s double name

Neither two nor one was called 41 .

Reason, in itself confounded 42 ,

Saw division grow together 43 ,

To themselves yet either neither 44 ,

Simple 45 were so well compounded 46 ,

That it 47 cried, ’How true 48 a twain

Seemeth this concordant 49 one!

Love hath reason, reason none,

If what parts can so remain 50 .’

Whereupon it made this threne 51

To the phoenix and the dove,

Co-supremes 52 and stars of love,

As chorus to their tragic scene.

THRENOS 53

Beauty, truth and rarity,

Grace in all simplicity,

Here enclosed in cinders lie.

Death is now the phoenix’ nest,

And the turtle’s loyal breast

To eternity doth rest,

Leaving no posterity 54 .

’Twas not their infirmity 55 ,

It was married chastity.

Truth may seem but cannot be 56 ,

Beauty brag but ’tis not she,

Truth and beauty buried be.

To this urn let those repair 57

That are either true or fair,

For these dead birds sigh a prayer.

William Shake-speare

The Phoenix: mythical bird that is the only one of its kind, perpetually regenerating itself from its own ashes, sometimes serving in the period as an image of Queen Elizabeth, but perhaps also suggesting the regenerative power of poetic art.
