NOTES

1 ‘Let … Lay’ untitled when originally published, this poem has been known since the early 1800s as ‘The Phoenix and Turtle’

2 bird … lay never positively identified within the poem; the following line suggests that it might refer to the mythological Arabian phoenix

3 lay song

4 trumpet trumpeter

5 shrieking harbinger often identified as the screech owl, regarded as a bird of ill omen whose cry heralded death

6 precurrer precursor

7 fiend devil

8 Augur predictor (literally soothsayer who made predictions based on the behaviour of birds)

9 session sitting (of court or parliament)

10 interdict prohibit

11 fowl … wing bird of prey/usurper, despot

12 Save except

13 obsequy funeral rites

14 strict restricted, exclusive/rigorously maintained

15 surplice ecclesiastical garment (here, the swan’s white feathers)

16 defunctive deathly, funereal

17 can knows

18 death-divining the swan was thought to know that its own death was coming, at which point it would sing

19 his right what is due (either to the swan or to the requiem; puns on ‘rite’)

20 treble-dated living three times the normal span

21 sable gender black offspring; the crow was thought to reproduce chastely through the touching of beaks and exchange of breath

22 anthem piece of music or song of praise

23 Phoenix mythological Arabian bird which lived for five hundred years, was consumed by fire and then reborn from the ashes; only one existed at a time

24 turtle turtle-dove, a bird renowned for love and constancy

25 fled have departed this life

26 In … flame together in one flame

27 So … as they so loved that

28 twain two

29 essence … one essential irreducible quality of one thing

30 distincts separate things

31 Number the concept of plurality

32 remote apart

33 asunder separated

34 Distance distance and proximity was seen

35 But … wonder in anyone other than them it would have been extraordinary

36 right due, what belonged to him

37 sight eyes, gaze

38 Either … mine they belonged to each other/each was the source of the other’s wealth

39 Property ownership/self-possession

40 the same itself

41 Single … called i.e. their nature was both separate and united, so it could not properly be called either one or two

42 confounded confused/overthrown

43 division grow together separateness resolve into one

44 To … neither i.e. each one was nothing without the other

45 Simple a single unmixed substance

46 compounded combined

47 it i.e. Reason

48 true truly/faithful

49 concordant harmonious

50 what … remain separate elements remain so united

51 threne threnody, song of lamentation for the dead

52 Co-supremes joint rulers

53 THRENOS threnody, funeral lament

54 posterity offspring

55 infirmity physical inability

56 Truth … be i.e. since both truth and beauty are buried with the phoenix and the turtle, whatever appears like them cannot really be so

57 repair make their way

Shakespeare’s Sonnets

TO THE ONLY BEGETTER

1 OF

THESE INSUING SONNETS

MR. W. H. 2 ALL HAPPINESS

AND THAT ETERNITY 3

PROMISED

BY

OUR EVER-LIVING POET 4

WISHETH

THE WELL-WISHING

ADVENTURER 5 IN

SETTING

FORTH

T. T. 6

1

FROM fairest creatures we desire increase 7 ,

That 8 thereby beauty’s rose might never die,

But as the riper should by time decease,

His tender 9 heir might bear his memory 10 :

But thou 11 , contracted 12 to thine own bright eyes 13 ,

Feed’st thy light’s flame with self-substantial fuel 14 ,

Making a famine where abundance lies,

Thyself thy foe, to thy sweet self too cruel.

Thou that art now the world’s fresh 15 ornament

And only herald 16 to the gaudy 17 spring,

Within thine own bud 18 buriest thy content 19

And, tender churl 20 , mak’st waste 21 in niggarding 22 .

Pity the world, or else this glutton be,

To eat the world’s due 23 , by the grave and thee 24 .

2

When forty 25 winters shall besiege thy brow

And dig deep trenches in thy beauty’s field 26 ,

Thy youth’s proud livery 27 , so gazed on now,

Will be a tattered weed 28 of small worth held:

Then being asked where all thy beauty lies,

Where all the treasure 29 of thy lusty 30 days,

To say within thine own deep-sunken eyes

Were an all-eating shame 31 and thriftless 32 praise.

How much more praise deserved thy beauty’s use 33

If thou couldst answer 34 , ’This fair child of mine

Shall sum my count 35 and make my old excuse 36 ’,

Proving his beauty by succession 37 thine 38 .

This were 39 to be new made when thou art old

And see thy blood 40 warm when thou feel’st it cold.

3

Look in thy glass 41 and tell the face thou viewest

Now is the time that face should form another,

Whose fresh repair 42 if now thou not renewest

Thou dost beguile 43 the world, unbless some mother 44 .

For where is she so fair whose uneared 45 womb

Disdains the tillage 46 of thy husbandry 47 ?

Or who is he so fond 48 will be 49 the tomb

Of his self-love, to stop 50 posterity 51 ?

Thou art thy mother’s glass and she in thee

Calls back 52 the lovely April of her prime:

So thou through windows 53 of thine age shalt see,

Despite of wrinkles, this thy golden time.

But if thou live remembered not to be 54 ,

Die 55 single and thine image dies with thee.

4

Unthrifty 56 loveliness, why dost thou spend 57

Upon thyself thy beauty’s legacy 58 ?

Nature’s bequest gives nothing but doth lend,

And being frank 59 she lends to those 60 are free 61 .

Then, beauteous niggard 62 , why dost thou abuse

The bounteous largesse given thee to give?

Profitless usurer 63 , why dost thou use 64

So great a sum of sums, yet canst not live 65 ?

For having traffic 66 with thyself alone,

Thou of thyself thy sweet self dost deceive 67 .

Then how, when nature calls thee to be gone,

What acceptable audit 68 canst thou leave?

Thy unused 69 beauty must be tombed 70 with thee,

Which, usèd, lives th’executor 71 to be.

5

Those hours, that with gentle work did frame 72

The lovely gaze 73 where every eye doth dwell 74 ,

Will play the tyrants to the very same

And that unfair 75 which fairly 76 doth excel:

For never-resting time leads 77 summer on

To hideous winter and confounds 78 him there,

Sap checked 79 with frost and lusty 80 leaves quite gone,

Beauty o’ersnowed 81 and bareness everywhere.

Then, were not summer’s distillation 82 left

A liquid prisoner pent in walls of glass 83 ,

Beauty’s effect with 84 beauty were bereft 85 ,

Nor it nor no remembrance what it was 86 .

But flowers distilled, though they with winter meet,

Lose but their show 87 , their substance still 88 lives sweet.

6

Then let not winter’s ragged 89 hand deface

In thee thy summer, ere thou be distilled 90 :

Make sweet some vial 91 ; treasure 92 thou some place 93

With beauty’s treasure, ere it be self-killed 94 .

That use 95 is not forbidden usury,

Which happies 96 those that pay the willing loan 97 ;

That’s for thyself to breed another thee,

Or ten times happier be it ten for one 98 .

Ten times thyself were happier than thou art,

If ten of thine ten times refigured 99 thee.

Then what could death do, if thou shouldst depart,

Leaving thee living in posterity 100 ?

Be not self-willed 101 , for thou art much too fair 102

To be death’s conquest and make worms thine heir.

7

Lo, in the orient 103 when the gracious light 104

Lifts up his burning head, each under 105 eye

Doth homage to his new-appearing sight,

Serving with looks his sacred majesty.

And having climbed the steep-up 106 heavenly hill 107 ,

Resembling strong youth in his middle age,

Yet mortal looks adore his beauty still,

Attending on his golden pilgrimage.

But when from highmost pitch 108 , with weary car 109 ,

Like feeble age he reeleth 110 from the day,

The eyes, fore 111 duteous, now converted 112 are

From his low tract 113 and look another way:

So thou, thyself outgoing 114 in thy noon 115 ,

Unlooked on diest 116 unless thou get 117 a son 118 .

8

Music to hear 119 , why hear’st thou music sadly 120 ?

Sweets with sweets war not, joy delights in joy.

Why lov’st thou that which thou receiv’st not gladly,

Or else receiv’st with pleasure thine annoy 121 ?

If the true concord of well-tunèd sounds,

By unions 122 married, do offend thine ear,

They do but sweetly chide 123 thee, who confounds 124

In singleness 125 the parts that thou shouldst bear 126 .

Mark 127 how one string, sweet husband to another,

Strikes each in each 128 by mutual ordering,

Resembling sire 129 and child and happy mother

Who, all in one, one pleasing note do sing,

Whose speechless 130 song, being many, seeming one,

Sings this to thee: ‘Thou single wilt prove none.’ 131

9

Is it for fear to wet a widow’s eye

That thou consum’st thyself in single life?

Ah, if thou issueless 132 shalt hap 133 to die,

The world will wail thee, like a makeless 134 wife,

The world will be thy widow and still 135 weep

That thou no form 136 of thee hast left behind,

When every private 137 widow well may keep

By 138 children’s eyes her husband’s shape in mind.

Look what 139 an unthrift 140 in the world doth spend

Shifts but his 141 place, for still the world enjoys it 142 ,

But beauty’s waste 143 hath in the world an end,

And, kept unused 144 , the user 145 so destroys it.

No love toward others in that bosom 146 sits

That on himself such murd’rous shame 147 commits.

10

For shame 148 deny that thou bear’st 149 love to any,

Who for thyself art so unprovident 150 .

Grant 151 , if thou wilt, thou art beloved of many,

But that thou none lov’st is most evident:

For thou art so possessed with murd’rous hate

That gainst thyself thou stick’st 152 not to conspire,

Seeking that beauteous roof 153 to ruinate

Which to repair should be thy chief desire.

O, change thy thought 154 , that I may change my mind 155 .

Shall hate be fairer lodged 156 than gentle love?

Be as thy presence 157 is, gracious and kind 158 ,

Or to thyself at least kind-hearted prove.

Make thee another self 159 , for love of me,

That beauty still 160 may live in thine or thee 161 .

11

As fast as thou shalt wane 162 , so fast thou grow’st

In one of thine 163 , from that which thou departest 164 ,

And that fresh blood 165 which youngly 166 thou bestow’st,

Thou mayst call thine when thou from youth convertest 167 168 .

Herein 169 lives wisdom, beauty and increase 170 :

Without this, folly, age and cold decay.

If all were minded so 171 , the times should cease,

And threescore year 172 would make the world away 173 .

Let those whom nature hath not made for store 174 ,

Harsh, featureless 175 and rude 176 , barrenly perish.

Look whom 177 she best endowed she gave the more,

Which bounteous gift thou shouldst in bounty 178 cherish.

She carved thee for her seal 179 , and meant thereby

Thou shouldst print 180 more, not let that copy 181 die.

12

When I do count 182 the clock that tells 183 the time

And see the brave 184 day sunk in hideous night,

When I behold the violet past prime 185

And sable 186 curls all silvered o’er with white,

When lofty trees I see barren 187 of leaves,

Which erst 188 from heat did canopy 189 the herd,

And summer’s green all girded up 190 in sheaves

Borne on the bier 191 with white and bristly beard 192 :

Then of thy beauty do I question make 193

That thou among the wastes 194 of time must go,

Since sweets and beauties 195 do themselves forsake 196

And die as fast as they see others grow,

And nothing gainst Time’s scythe 197 can make defence

Save breed 198 , to brave 199 him when he takes thee hence.

13

O, that you 200 were yourself! But, love, you are

No longer yours than you yourself here 201 live.

Against 202 this coming end you should prepare

And your sweet semblance 203 to some other 204 give.

So should that beauty which you hold in lease 205

Find no determination 206 : then you were 207

Yourself again after yourself’s decease,

When your sweet issue 208 your sweet form should bear 209 .

Who lets so fair a house 210 fall to decay,

Which husbandry 211 in honour might uphold

Against the stormy gusts of winter’s day

And barren rage of death’s eternal cold?

O, none but unthrifts 212 ! Dear my love, you know

You had a father: let your son say so.

14

Not from the stars do I my judgement pluck 213

And yet methinks I have 214 astronomy,

But not to tell of good or evil luck,

Of plagues, of dearths 215 , or seasons’ quality 216 ,

Nor can I fortune to brief minutes tell 217 ,

Pointing to each his thunder, rain and wind,

Or say with princes if it shall go well,

By oft predict 218 that I in heaven find.

But from thine eyes my knowledge I derive,

And, constant stars, in them I read such art 219

As 220 truth and beauty shall together thrive,

If from thyself to store 221 thou wouldst convert 222 :

Or else of thee this I prognosticate 223 ,

Thy end is truth’s and beauty’s doom and date 224 225 .

15

When I consider 226 everything that grows

Holds in perfection 227 but a little moment,

That this huge stage 228 presenteth nought but shows

Whereon the stars in secret influence 229 comment 230 :

When I perceive that men as 231 plants increase,

Cheerèd and checked 232 ev’n by the selfsame sky,

Vaunt 233 in their youthful sap, at height decrease 234 ,

And wear their brave state out of memory 235 :

Then the conceit 236 of this inconstant stay 237

Sets you most rich in youth before my sight,

Where wasteful 238 time debateth with decay 239

To change your day of youth to sullied 240 night,

And all in war 241 with Time for love of you,

As he takes from you, I engraft you new 242 .

16

But wherefore 243 do not you a mightier way

Make war upon this bloody 244 tyrant, Time?

And fortify 245 yourself in your decay

With means more blessèd than my barren rhyme?

Now stand you on the top of happy hours 246 ,

And many maiden 247 gardens 248 , yet unset 249 ,

With virtuous wish would bear your living flowers,

Much liker 250 than your painted counterfeit 251 :

So should the lines of life 252 that life repair 253 ,

Which this 254 , Time’s pencil 255 , or my pupil 256 pen,

Neither in inward worth nor outward fair 257 ,

Can make you live yourself 258 in eyes of men.

To give away yourself 259 keeps yourself still 260 ,

And you must live, drawn by your own sweet skill 261 .

17

Who will believe my verse in time to come

If it were filled with your most high deserts 262 ?

Though yet, heaven knows, it is but as a tomb

Which hides your life and shows not half your parts 263 .

If I could write the beauty of your eyes

And in fresh number 264 s number all your graces,

The age to come would say, ’This poet lies:

Such heavenly touches 265 ne’er touched earthly faces.’

So should my papers, yellowed with their age,

Be scorned like old men of less truth than tongue 266 ,

And your true 267 rights 268 be termed a poet’s rage 269

And stretchèd 270 metre of an antique 271 song.

But were some child of yours alive that time,

You should live twice — in it and in my rhyme.

18

Shall I compare thee to a summer’s day?

Thou art more lovely and more temperate 272 .

Rough winds do shake the darling buds of May,

And summer’s lease 273 hath all too short a date 274 .

Sometime too hot the eye of heaven 275 shines,

And often is his gold complexion dimmed,

And every fair from fair sometime declines 276 ,

By chance or nature’s changing course untrimmed 277 :

But thy eternal summer shall not fade

Nor lose possession 278 of that fair thou ow’st 279 ,

Nor shall death brag thou wand’rest in his shade,

When in eternal lines 280 to time thou grow’st 281 .

So long as men can breathe or eyes can see,

So long lives this 282 and this gives life to thee.

19

Devouring Time, blunt thou the lion’s paws,

And make the earth devour her own sweet brood 283 ,

Pluck the keen 284 teeth from the fierce tiger’s jaws

And burn the long-lived phoenix 285 in her blood,

Make glad and sorry seasons as thou fleet’st 286

And do whate’er thou wilt, swift-footed Time,

To the wide world and all her fading sweets 287 .

But I forbid thee one most heinous crime:

O, carve 288 not with thy hours my love’s fair brow,

Nor draw no lines there with thine antique 289 pen.

Him in thy course untainted 290 do allow

For beauty’s pattern 291 to succeeding men.

Yet do thy worst, old Time: despite thy wrong,

My love 292 shall in my verse ever live young.

20 293

A woman’s face with Nature’s own hand painted 294

Hast thou, the master-mistress 295 of my passion 296 ,

A woman’s gentle heart, but not acquainted 297

With shifting change as is false 298 women’s fashion,

An eye more bright than theirs, less false in rolling 299 ,

Gilding 300 the object whereupon it gazeth:

A man in hue 301 , all hues in his controlling 302 ,

Which steals men’s eyes and women’s souls amazeth.

And for 303 a woman wert thou first created,

Till Nature as she wrought 304 thee fell a-doting 305 ,

And by addition me of thee defeated 306

By adding one thing 307 to my purpose nothing 308 .

But since she pricked thee out 309 for women’s pleasure 310 ,

Mine be thy love and thy love’s use 311 their treasure 312 313 .

21

So is it not with me as with that Muse 314 ,

Stirred 315 by a painted 316 beauty to his verse,

Who heaven itself for ornament doth use 317

And every fair with his fair doth rehearse 318 ,

Making a couplement of proud compare 319

With sun and moon, with earth and sea’s rich gems,

With April’s first-born flowers and all things rare 320

That heaven’s air in this huge rondure 321 hems 322 .

O let me, true in love, but truly write,

And then believe me, my love is as fair

As any mother’s child 323 , though not so bright

As those gold candles 324 fixed in heaven’s air.

Let them say more that like of hearsay 325 well:

I will not praise that purpose 326 not to sell.

22

My glass 327 shall not persuade me I am old,

So long as youth and thou are of one date 328 ,

But when in thee time’s furrows 329 I behold,

Then look I 330 death my days should expiate 331 .

For all that beauty that doth cover thee

Is but the seemly raiment 332 of my heart,

Which in thy breast doth live, as thine in me 333 :

How can I then be elder than thou art?

O, therefore, love, be of thyself so wary 334

As I, not for myself, but for thee will,

Bearing thy heart, which I will keep so chary 335

As tender nurse her babe from faring ill.

Presume not on thy heart 336 when mine is slain:

Thou gav’st me thine, not to give back again.

23

As an unperfect 337 actor on the stage,

Who with his fear is put besides his part 338 ,

Or some fierce thing replete 339 with too much rage,

Whose strength’s abundance weakens his own heart,

So I, for fear of trust 340 , forget to say

The perfect ceremony of love’s rite 341 ,

And in mine own love’s strength seem to decay,

O’ercharged 342 with burden of mine own love’s might 343 .

O, let my books 344 be then the eloquence

And dumb presagers 345 of my speaking breast 346 ,

Who 347 plead for love and look for recompense,

More than that tongue 348 that more hath more expressed.

O, learn to read what silent love hath writ:

To hear with eyes belongs to love’s fine wit 349 .

24

Mine eye hath played the painter and hath stelled 350

Thy beauty’s form in table 351 of my heart,

My body is the frame 352 wherein ’tis held

And perspective 353 it is best painter’s art.

For through 354 the painter must you see his skill

To find where your true image pictured lies,

Which in my bosom’s shop 355 is hanging still 356 ,

That hath his windows glazèd with thine eyes 357 .

Now see what good turns eyes for eyes have done:

Mine eyes have drawn thy shape and thine for me

Are windows to my breast, wherethrough the sun

Delights to peep, to gaze therein on thee.

Yet eyes this cunning want 358 to grace their art:

They draw but what they see, know 359 not the heart.

25

Let those who are in favour with their stars

Of 360 public honour and proud titles boast,

Whilst I, whom fortune of such triumph 361 bars,

Unlooked for 362 joy in that I honour most 363 .

Great princes’ favourites their fair leaves 364 spread

But as the marigold 365 at the sun’s eye,

And in themselves their pride lies burièd 366 ,

For at a frown they in their glory 367 die.

The painful 368 warrior famousèd 369 for might 370 ,

After a thousand victories once foiled 371 ,

Is from the book of honour razèd 372 quite

And all the rest forgot for which he toiled.

Then happy I, that love and am beloved

Where I may not remove 373 nor be removed.

26

Lord of my love, to whom in vassalage 374

Thy merit hath my duty strongly knit 375 ,

To thee I send this written ambassage 376

To witness 377 duty, not to show my wit 378 .

Duty so great, which wit so poor as mine

May make seem bare, in wanting 379 words to show it,

But that I hope some good conceit of thine

In thy soul’s thought, all naked, will bestow it 380 ,

Till whatsoever star that guides my moving 381

Points on me graciously with fair aspect 382

And puts apparel on my tattered loving

To show me worthy of thy sweet respect.

Then may I dare to boast how I do love thee:

Till then not show my head where thou mayst prove me 383 .

27

Weary with toil, I haste me to my bed,

The dear 384 repose for limbs with travel 385 tired,

But then begins a journey in my head,

To work my mind, when body’s work’s expired:

For then my thoughts, from far 386 where I abide,

Intend 387 a zealous pilgrimage to thee,

And keep my drooping eyelids open wide,

Looking on darkness which the blind do see,

Save that my soul’s imaginary sight

Presents thy shadow 388 to my sightless view,

Which 389 , like a jewel hung in ghastly 390 night,

Makes black night beauteous and her old face new.

Lo, thus, by day my limbs, by night my mind,

For 391 thee and for myself no quiet find.

28

How can I then 392 return in happy plight 393

That am debarred the benefit of rest,

When day’s oppression 394 is not eased by night,

But day by night and night by day oppressed?

And each, though enemies to either’s reign,

Do in consent shake hands 395 to torture me,

The one by toil, the other to complain 396

How far I toil, still further off from thee.

I tell the day, to please him thou art bright

And dost him grace 397 when clouds do blot the heaven:

So flatter I the swart-complexioned 398 night,

When sparkling stars twire 399 not thou gild’st the even 400 .

But day doth daily draw my sorrows longer,

And night doth nightly make grief’s length seem stronger.

29

When in disgrace 401 with Fortune and men’s eyes,

I all alone beweep 402 my outcast state

And trouble deaf heaven with my bootless 403 cries

And look upon myself and curse my fate,

Wishing me like to one more rich in hope,

Featured like him, like him with friends possessed 404 ,

Desiring this man’s art 405 and that man’s scope 406 ,

With what I most enjoy contented least 407 :

Yet in these thoughts myself almost despising,

Haply 408 I think on thee and then my state 409 ,

Like to the lark at break of day arising,

From sullen 410 earth sings hymns at heaven’s gate,

For thy sweet love remembered such wealth brings

That then I scorn to change 411 my state with kings.

30

When to the sessions of sweet silent thought

I summon 412 up remembrance of things past,

I sigh 413 the lack of many a thing I sought

And with old woes new wail 414 my dear 415 time’s waste.

Then can I drown an eye, unused to flow 416 ,

For precious friends hid in death’s dateless 417 night,

And weep afresh love’s long since cancelled 418 woe,

And moan th’expense 419 of many a vanished sight.

Then can I grieve at grievances foregone 420 ,

And heavily 421 from woe to woe tell o’er 422

The sad account 423 of fore-bemoanèd moan 424 ,

Which I new pay as if not paid before.

But if the while I think on thee, dear friend,

All losses are restored and sorrows end.

31

Thy bosom 425 is endearèd with 426 all hearts,

Which I by lacking 427 have supposèd dead 428 ,

And there reigns love and all love’s loving parts 429

And all those friends which I thought burièd.

How many a holy and obsequious 430 tear

Hath dear 431 religious 432 love stol’n from mine eye

As interest 433 of the dead, which now appear

But things removed 434 that hidden in there 435 lie.

Thou art the grave where buried love doth live,

Hung with the trophies 436 of my lovers gone,

Who all their parts of me to thee did give 437 :

That due of 438 many now is thine alone.

Their images I loved 439 I view in thee,

And thou, all they, hast all the all of me 440 .

32

If thou survive my well-contented day 441 ,

When that churl 442 death my bones with dust shall cover,

And shalt by fortune 443 once more resurvey

These poor rude 444 lines of thy deceasèd lover,

Compare them with the bett’ring of the time 445 ,

And though they be outstripped by every pen,

Reserve 446 them for my love, not for their rhyme 447 ,

Exceeded by the height 448 of happier 449 men.

O, then vouchsafe 450 me but this loving thought:

’Had my friend’s Muse 451 grown with this growing age,

A dearer birth 452 than this his love had brought

To march in ranks of better equipage 453 ,

But since he died and poets better prove,

Theirs for their style I’ll read, his for his love.’

33

Full many a glorious morning have I seen

Flatter the mountain tops with sovereign eye,

Kissing with golden face the meadows green,

Gilding pale streams with heavenly alchemy 454 ,

Anon 455 permit the basest 456 clouds to ride

With ugly rack 457 on his celestial face,

And from the forlorn world his visage 458 hide,

Stealing unseen to west with this disgrace:

Even so my sun one early morn did shine

With all-triumphant 459 splendour on my brow:

But out, alack 460 , he was but one hour mine,

The region cloud 461 hath masked him from me now.

Yet him for this my love no whit 462 disdaineth:

Suns of the world 463 may stain 464 when heaven’s sun staineth.

34

Why didst thou 465 promise such a beauteous day

And make me travel forth without my cloak,

To let base clouds o’ertake me in my way,

Hiding thy brav’ry 466 in their rotten smoke 467 ?

’Tis not enough that through the cloud thou break

To dry the rain on my storm-beaten face,

For no man well of such a salve 468 can speak

That heals the wound and cures not the disgrace 469 .

Nor can thy shame give physic 470 to my grief:

Though thou repent, yet I have still the loss.

Th’offender’s sorrow lends but weak relief

To him that bears the strong offence’s cross 471 .

Ah, but those tears are pearl, which thy love sheds,

And they are rich and ransom 472 all ill deeds.

35

No more be grieved at that which thou hast done:

Roses have thorns and silver fountains mud,

Clouds and eclipses stain 473 both moon and sun,

And loathsome canker 474 lives in sweetest bud.

All men make faults and even I in this,

Authorizing thy trespass with compare 475 ,

Myself corrupting 476 , salving 477 thy amiss 478 ,

Excusing thy sins more than thy sins are 479 :

For to thy sensual fault I bring in sense 480 —

Thy adverse party is thy advocate 481 —

And gainst myself a lawful plea commence 482 .

Such civil war is in my love and hate

That I an accessory 483 needs must be

To that sweet thief which sourly robs from me.

36

Let me confess that we two must be twain 484 ,

Although our undivided loves are one:

So shall those blots 485 that do with me remain,

Without thy help by me be borne alone.

In our two loves there is but one respect 486 ,

Though in our lives a separable spite 487 ,

Which though it alter not love’s sole 488 effect,

Yet doth it steal sweet hours from love’s delight.

I may not evermore acknowledge thee 489 ,

Lest my bewailèd guilt 490 should do thee shame,

Nor thou with public kindness honour me,

Unless thou take that honour from thy name 491 .

But do not so: I love thee in such sort 492

As, thou being mine, mine is thy good report 493 .

37

As a decrepit father takes delight

To see his active child do deeds of youth,

So I, made lame by Fortune’s dearest spite 494 ,

Take all my comfort of 495 thy worth and truth.

For whether beauty, birth or wealth or wit 496 ,

Or any of these all or all or more,

Entitled in thy parts do crownèd sit 497 ,

I make my love engrafted to this store 498 :

So then I am not lame, poor, nor despised,

Whilst that this shadow 499 doth such substance give

That I in thy abundance am sufficed 500

And by a part of all thy glory live.

Look what 501 is best, that best I wish in thee.

This wish I have: then ten times happy me.

38

How can my Muse 502 want subject to invent 503 ,

While thou dost breathe, that pour’st into my verse

Thine own sweet argument 504 , too excellent

For every vulgar paper to rehearse 505 ?

O, give thyself the thanks, if aught 506 in me

Worthy perusal 507 stand against thy sight 508 ,

For who’s so dumb 509 that cannot write to thee,

When thou thyself dost give invention light 510 ?

Be thou the tenth Muse 511 , ten times more in worth

Than those old nine which rhymers invocate 512 ,

And he that calls on thee, let him bring forth

Eternal numbers to outlive long date 513 .

If my slight Muse 514 do please these curious 515 days,

The pain 516 be mine, but thine shall be the praise.

39

O, how thy worth 517 with manners 518 may I sing,

When thou art all the better part of me?

What can mine own praise to mine own self bring?

And what is’t but mine own when I praise thee?

Even for 519 this let us divided live,

And our dear 520 love lose name of single one,

That 521 by this separation I may give

That due to thee which thou deserv’st alone.

O absence, what a torment wouldst thou prove,

Were it not thy sour leisure gave sweet leave

To entertain 522 the time with thoughts of love,

Which time and thoughts so sweetly dost deceive 523 ,

And that thou teachest how to make one twain 524

By praising him here 525 who doth hence 526 remain.

40

Take 527 all my loves 528 , my love, yea, take them all:

What hast thou then more than thou hadst before?

No love, my love, that thou mayst true love call:

All mine was thine before thou hadst this more.

Then if for my love 529 thou my love receivest 530 ,

I cannot blame thee for my love thou usest 531 .

But yet be blamed, if thou this self 532 deceivest

By wilful taste 533 of what thyself refusest 534 .

I do forgive thy robb’ry, gentle thief,

Although thou steal thee 535 all my poverty 536 :

And yet love knows it is a greater grief

To bear love’s wrong 537 than hate’s known injury.

Lascivious grace 538 , in whom all ill well shows 539 ,

Kill me with spites 540 , yet we must not be foes.

41

Those pretty wrongs that liberty 541 commits

When I am sometime absent from thy heart,

Thy beauty and thy years full well befits 542 ,

For still 543 temptation follows where thou art.

Gentle 544 thou art and therefore to be won,

Beauteous thou art, therefore to be assailed 545 .

And when a woman woos, what woman’s son

Will sourly 546 leave her till he have prevailed 547 ?

Ay me, but yet thou mightst my seat 548 forbear 549

And chide 550 thy beauty and thy straying 551 youth,

Who lead thee in their riot 552 even there 553

Where thou art forced to break a two-fold truth 554 :

Hers 555 by thy beauty tempting her to thee,

Thine 556 by thy beauty being false 557 to me.

42

That thou hast her, it is not all my grief,

And yet it may be said I loved her dearly,

That she hath thee is of my wailing chief 558 ,

A loss in love that touches me more nearly.

Loving offenders 559 , thus I will excuse ye:

Thou dost love her, because thou knowst I love her,

And for my sake even so 560 doth she abuse 561 me,

Suff’ring 562 my friend for my sake to approve 563 her.

If I lose thee, my loss is my love’s 564 gain,

And losing her, my friend hath found that loss:

Both find each other and I lose both twain 565 ,

And both for my sake lay on me this cross 566 .

But here’s the joy: my friend and I are one.

Sweet flatt’ry 567 ! Then she loves but me alone.

43

When most I wink 568 , then do mine eyes best see,

For all the day they view things unrespected 569 ,

But when I sleep, in dreams they look on thee

And, darkly bright, are bright in dark directed 570 .

Then thou, whose shadow shadows doth make bright 571 ,

How would thy shadow’s form form happy show 572

To the clear day with thy much clearer light,

When to unseeing eyes thy shade 573 shines so?

How would, I say, mine eyes be blessèd made

By looking on thee in the living 574 day,

When in dead night thy fair imperfect 575 shade

Through heavy sleep on sightless eyes doth stay?

All days are nights to see 576 till I see thee,

And nights bright days when dreams do show thee me.

44

If the dull 577 substance of my flesh were thought,

Injurious 578 distance should not stop my way,

For then despite 579 of space I would be brought,

From limits 580 far remote, where 581 thou dost stay.

No matter 582 then, although my foot did stand

Upon the farthest earth removed from thee,

For nimble thought can jump both sea and land

As soon as think the place where he would be.

But ah, thought kills me that I am not thought,

To leap large lengths of miles when thou art gone,

But that so much of earth and water wrought 583 ,

I must attend time’s leisure 584 with my moan,

Receiving naught by elements so slow

But heavy 585 tears, badges of either’s woe 586 .

45

The other two 587 , slight 588 air and purging 589 fire,

Are both with thee, wherever I abide:

The first 590 my thought, the other 591 my desire,

These present-absent 592 with swift motion slide.

For when these quicker 593 elements are gone

In tender embassy 594 of love to thee,

My life, being made of four, with two alone 595

Sinks down to death, oppressed with melancholy 596 ,

Until life’s composition be recured 597

By those swift messengers returned from thee,

Who even but now 598 come back again, assured

Of thy fair health, recounting it to me.

This told, I joy 599 — but then no longer glad,

I send them back again and straight 600 grow sad.

46

Mine eye and heart are at a mortal 601 war

How to divide the conquest of thy sight 602 :

Mine eye my heart thy picture’s sight would bar 603 ,

My heart mine eye the freedom of that right 604 .

My heart doth plead 605 that thou in him dost lie —

A closet 606 never pierced with crystal eyes —

But the defendant 607 doth that plea deny

And says in him thy fair appearance lies.

To ’cide 608 this title 609 is empannellèd 610

A quest 611 of thoughts, all tenants 612 to the heart,

And by their verdict is determinèd 613

The clear eye’s moiety 614 and the dear heart’s part,

As thus 615 : mine eye’s due is thy outward part 616 ,

And my heart’s right thy inward love of heart.

47

Betwixt mine eye and heart a league 617 is took,

And each doth good turns now unto the other:

When that 618 mine eye is famished for a look,

Or heart 619 in love with sighs himself 620 doth smother,

With 621 my love’s picture then my eye doth feast

And to the painted banquet bids 622 my heart.

Another time mine eye is my heart’s guest

And in his thoughts of love doth share a part.

So, either by thy picture or my love,

Thyself away are present still 623 with me,

For thou not further than my thoughts canst move,

And I am still with them and they with thee.

Or, if they sleep, thy picture in my sight

Awakes my heart to heart’s and eye’s delight.

48

How careful was I, when I took my way 624 ,

Each trifle under truest bars 625 to thrust,

That to my use 626 it might unusèd stay

From hands of falsehood 627 , in sure 628 wards 629 of trust.

But thou, to whom 630 my jewels trifles are,

Most worthy comfort, now my greatest grief,

Thou, best of dearest and mine only care 631 ,

Art left the prey of every vulgar 632 thief.

Thee have I not locked up in any chest,

Save where thou art not, though I feel thou art,

Within the gentle closure 633 of my breast,

From whence at pleasure 634 thou mayst come and part:

And even thence thou wilt be stol’n, I fear,

For truth 635 proves thievish for a prize so dear 636 .

Against 637 that time, if ever that time come,

Doth teach that ease and that repose to say,

The beast 653 that bears me, tirèd with my woe,

Plods dully 654 on, to bear that weight in me,

As if by some instinct the wretch 655 did know

His rider loved not speed, being made 656 from thee:

The bloody spur cannot provoke him on

That sometimes anger thrusts into his hide,

Which heavily 657 he answers with a groan,

More sharp to me than spurring to his side,

For that same groan doth put this in my mind:

My grief lies onward and my joy behind.

51

Thus can my love excuse the slow offence 658

Of my dull bearer 659 when from thee I speed:

Till I return, of posting 660 is no need.

O, what excuse will my poor beast then find,

When swift extremity 661 can seem but slow?

Then should I spur 662 , though mounted on the wind:

In wingèd speed no motion shall I know 663 .

Then can no horse with my desire keep pace:

Therefore desire, of perfects 664 love being made,

Shall neigh — no dull flesh 665 — in his fiery race,

But love, for love 666 , thus shall excuse my jade 667 ,

Since from thee go 668 ing he went wilful-slow,

Towards thee I’ll run and give him leave to go.

So am I as the rich 669 , whose blessèd key

Is poorly imitated after you.

O, how much more doth beauty beauteous seem

The rose looks fair, but fairer we it deem 699

But, for their virtue only is their show 705 ,

They live unwooed 706 and unrespected 707 fade,

Die to themselves 708 . Sweet roses do not so:

Of their sweet deaths are sweetest odours made 709 .

And so of you, beauteous and lovely youth,

When that 710 shall fade, my verse distils your truth.

Being your slave, what should I do but tend 738

I have no precious time at all to spend,

Nor dare I chide 739 the world-without-end 740 hour

It fears not policy 1564 , that heretic 1565 ,

Which works on leases of short-numbered hours 1566 ,

But all alone stands hugely politic 1567 ,

That it nor grows with heat nor 1568 drowns with showers.

To this I witness 1569 call the fools 1570 of time 1571 ,

Which die for goodness 1572 , who have lived for crime.

125

Were’t aught to me 1573 I bore the canopy 1574 ,

With my extern the outward honouring 1575 ,

Or laid great bases 1576 for eternity 1577 ,

Which 1578 proves more short than waste or ruining?

Have I not seen dwellers on form and favour 1579

Lose all and more, by paying too much rent 1580 ,

For compound sweet forgoing simple savour 1581 ,

Pitiful thrivers 1582 , in their gazing spent 1583 ?

No, let me be obsequious 1584 in thy heart,

And take thou my oblation 1585 , poor but free,

Which is not mixed with seconds 1586 , knows no art 1587 ,

But mutual render 1588 , only me for thee.

Hence, thou suborned informer 1589 , a true soul

When most impeached 1590 stands least in thy control.

126

O thou, my lovely boy, who in thy power

Dost hold Time’s fickle glass 1591 , his sickle hour 1592 ,

Who hast by waning grown 1593 , and therein show’st

Thy lovers withering as thy sweet self grow’st —

If Nature, sovereign mistress over wrack 1594 ,

As thou goest onwards 1595 , still will pluck thee back,

She keeps thee to this purpose, that her skill

May Time disgrace 1596 and wretched minutes kill 1597 .

Yet fear her, O thou minion 1598 of her pleasure:

She may detain, but not still keep 1599 , her treasure.

Her audit 1600 , though delayed, answered 1601 must be,

And her quietus 1602 is to render 1603 thee.

( ) 1604

127

In the old age 1605 black 1606 was not counted fair 1607 ,

Or if it were, it bore not beauty’s name 1608 .

But now is black beauty’s successive 1609 heir

And beauty slandered with a bastard shame 1610 :

For since each hand hath put on 1611 nature’s power,

Fairing the foul 1612 with art’s false borrowed face,

Sweet beauty hath no name 1613 , no holy bower,

But is profaned 1614 , if not lives in disgrace.

Therefore my mistress’ brows are raven black,

Her eyes so suited 1615 , and they mourners seem

At such who, not born fair, no beauty lack 1616 ,

Sland’ring creation with a false esteem 1617 .

Yet so they mourn, becoming of their woe 1618 ,

That every tongue says beauty should look so.

128

How oft, when thou, my music 1619 , music play’st

Upon that blessèd wood 1620 whose motion sounds 1621

With thy sweet fingers, when thou gently sway’st 1622

The wiry concord 1623 that mine ear confounds 1624 ,

Do I envy those jacks 1625 that nimble leap

To kiss the tender inward of thy hand,

Whilst my poor lips, which should that harvest 1626 reap,

At the wood’s boldness by thee blushing stand.

To be so tickled, they would change their state

And situation with those dancing chips 1627

O’er whom thy fingers walk with gentle gait,

Making dead wood more blest than living lips.

Since saucy 1628 jacks so happy are in this,

Give them thy fingers, me thy lips to kiss.

129

Th’expense 1629 of spirit 1630 in a waste of shame 1631

Is lust in action 1632 , and till action, lust

Is perjured 1633 , murd’rous, bloody, full of blame 1634 ,

Savage, extreme, rude 1635 , cruel, not to trust 1636 ,

Enjoyed no sooner but despisèd straight 1637 ,

Past reason 1638 hunted and, no sooner had,

Past reason hated as a swallowed bait

On purpose laid to make the taker mad,

Mad in pursuit and in possession so,

Had, having and in quest to have, extreme,

A bliss in proof 1639 and proved 1640 a very woe,

Before, a joy proposed 1641 — behind 1642 , a dream 1643 .

All this the world well knows, yet none knows well

To shun the heaven that leads men to this hell 1644 .

130

My mistress’ eyes are nothing like the sun 1645 ,

Coral is far more red than her lips’ red,

If snow be white, why then her breasts are dun 1646 ,

If hairs be wires 1647 , black wires grow on her head.

I have seen roses damasked 1648 , red and white,

But no such roses see I in her cheeks,

And in some perfumes is there more delight

Than in the breath that from my mistress reeks 1649 .

I love to hear her speak, yet well I know

That music hath a far more pleasing sound.

I grant I never saw a goddess go 1650 :

My mistress when she walks treads on the ground.

And yet, by heaven, I think my love as rare

As any she belied with false compare 1651 .

131

Thou art as tyrannous 1652 , so as thou art 1653 ,

As those whose beauties proudly make them cruel,

For well thou know’st to my dear 1654 doting heart

Thou art the fairest and most precious jewel.

Yet in good faith 1655 some say, that thee behold,

Thy face hath not the power to make love groan 1656 .

To say they err I dare not be so bold,

Although I swear it to myself alone.

And to be sure that is not false I swear

A thousand groans but 1657 thinking on thy face

One on another’s neck 1658 do witness bear

Thy black 1659 is fairest 1660 in my judgement’s place 1661 .

In nothing art thou black save in thy deeds,

And thence this slander 1662 as I think proceeds.

132

Thine eyes I love and they, as pitying me,

Knowing thy heart torment me with disdain,

Have put on black and loving mourners be,

Looking with pretty ruth 1663 upon my pain.

And truly not the morning 1664 sun of heaven

Better becomes 1665 the grey cheeks of the east,

Nor that full star 1666 that ushers in the even 1667

Doth half that glory to the sober 1668 west,

As those two mourning 1669 eyes become thy face:

O, let it then as well beseem 1670 thy heart

To mourn for me, since mourning doth thee grace 1671 ,

And suit thy pity like in every part 1672 .

Then will I swear beauty herself is black,

And all they foul 1673 that thy complexion lack.

133

Beshrew 1674 that heart that makes my heart to groan

For that deep wound 1675 it gives my friend and me.

Is’t not enough to torture me alone,

But slave to slavery 1676 my sweet’st friend must be?

Me from myself thy cruel eye 1677 hath taken,

And my next self 1678 thou harder 1679 hast engrossed 1680 .

Of 1681 him, myself and thee, I am forsaken 1682 ,

A torment thrice threefold thus to be crossed 1683 .

Prison 1684 my heart in thy steel bosom’s ward 1685 ,

But then my friend’s heart let my poor heart bail 1686 ,

Whoe’er keeps 1687 me, let my heart be his 1688 guard:

Thou canst not then use rigour 1689 in my jail.

And yet thou wilt, for I, being pent 1690 in thee,

Perforce am thine and all that is in me 1691 .

134

So, now I have confessed that he 1692 is thine,

And I myself am mortgaged to thy will 1693 ,

Myself I’ll forfeit, so that other mine 1694

Thou wilt restore, to be my comfort still:

But thou wilt not, nor he will not be free,

For thou art covetous and he is kind 1695 .

He learned but surety-like to write for me 1696

Under that bond 1697 that him as fast 1698 doth bind.

The statute 1699 of thy beauty thou wilt take,

Thou usurer 1700 , that put’st forth all to use 1701 ,

And sue 1702 a friend came debtor 1703 for my sake:

So him I lose through my unkind abuse 1704 .

Him have I lost, thou hast 1705 both him and me:

He pays the whole 1706 , and yet am I not free.

135

Whoever hath her wish, thou hast thy Will 1707 ,

And Will to boot 1708 , and Will in overplus 1709 :

More than enough am I that vex 1710 thee still 1711 ,

To thy sweet will making addition 1712 thus.

Wilt thou, whose will is large and spacious 1713 ,

Not once vouchsafe 1714 to hide my will in thine 1715 ?

Shall will in others seem right gracious 1716 ,

And in my will no fair acceptance shine 1717 ?

The sea all water, yet receives rain still

And in abundance addeth to his store.

So thou, being rich in Will 1718 , add to thy Will 1719

One will of mine 1720 , to make thy large Will more 1721 .

Let no unkind, no fair beseechers kill 1722 :

Think all but one and me in that one Will 1723 .

136

If thy soul check 1724 thee that I come so near 1725 ,

Swear to thy blind 1726 soul that I was thy Will 1727 ,

And will, thy soul knows, is admitted there 1728 :

Thus far for love 1729 my love-suit 1730 , sweet, fulfil.

Will 1731 will fulfil 1732 the treasure of thy love 1733 ,

Ay, fill it full with wills, and my will one 1734 .

In things of great receipt 1735 with ease we prove

Among a number one is reckoned none.

Then in the number let me pass untold 1736 ,

Though in thy store’s account 1737 I one must be:

For nothing hold me 1738 , so 1739 it please thee hold 1740

That nothing me 1741 , a something 1742 sweet to thee.

Make but 1743 my name 1744 thy love and love that still 1745 ,

And then thou lovest me, for my name is Will.

137

Thou blind 1746 fool, love, what dost thou to mine eyes,

That they behold and see not what they see?

They know what beauty is, see where it lies 1747 ,

Yet what the be 1748 st is take the worst to be.

If eyes corrupt by over-partial looks 1749

Be anchored in the bay 1750 where all men ride 1751 ,

Why of eyes’ falsehood hast thou forgèd hooks,

Whereto the judgement of my heart is tied?

Why should my heart think that a several plot 1752

Which my heart knows the wide world’s common place 1753 ?

Or 1754 mine eyes seeing this, say this is not 1755 ,

To 1756 put fair truth upon so foul a face?

In things right true my heart and eyes have erred,

And to this false plague 1757 are they now transferred.

138 1758

When my love swears that she is made of truth 1759 ,

I do believe her, though I know she lies 1760 ,

That 1761 she might think me some untutored youth,

Unlearnèd in the world’s false subtleties 1762 .

Thus vainly 1763 thinking that she thinks me young,

Although she knows my days are past the best,

Simply 1764 I credit 1765 her false-speaking tongue:

On both sides thus is simple truth suppressed.

But wherefore 1766 says she not she is unjust 1767 ?

And wherefore say not I that I am old?

O, love’s best habit 1768 is in seeming 1769 trust,

And age in love loves not to have years told 1770 .

Therefore I lie 1771 with her and she with me,

And in our faults by lies we flattered 1772 be.

139

O, call not me to justify the wrong

That thy unkindness lays upon my heart,

Wound me not with thine eye but with thy tongue 1773 ,

Use power with power 1774 and slay me not by art 1775 ,

Tell me thou lov’st elsewhere, but in my sight,

Dear heart, forbear to glance thine eye aside.

What need’st thou wound with cunning when thy might 1776

Is more than my o’erpressed 1777 defence can bide 1778 ?

Let me excuse thee: ah, my love well knows

Her pretty looks 1779 have been mine enemies,

And therefore from my face she turns my foes,

That they elsewhere might dart their injuries:

Yet do not so, but since I am near slain,

Kill me outright with looks and rid my pain.

140

Be wise as thou art cruel, do not press 1780

My tongue-tied patience with too much disdain,

Lest sorrow lend me words and words express

The manner of my pity-wanting pain 1781 .

If I might teach thee wit 1782 , better it were,

Though not to love, yet, love, to tell me so,

As testy 1783 sick men, when their deaths be near,

No news but health from their physicians know 1784 .

For if I should despair, I should grow mad,

And in my madness might speak ill of thee.

Now this ill-wresting world 1785 is grown so 1786 bad,

Mad slanderers by mad ears believèd be.

That I may not be so, nor thou belied 1787 ,

Bear thine eyes straight 1788 , though thy proud heart go wide 1789 .

141

In faith, I do not love thee with mine eyes,

For they in thee a thousand errors 1790 note,

But ’tis my heart that loves what they despise,

Who in despite of 1791 view is pleased to dote.

Nor are mine ears with thy tongue’s tune delighted,

Nor tender feeling to base touches 1792 prone,

Nor taste, nor smell, desire to be invited

To any sensual feast 1793 with thee alone.

But my five wits 1794 nor my five senses can

Dissuade one foolish heart from serving thee,

Who leaves unswayed the likeness of a man 1795 ,

Thy proud heart’s slave and vassal 1796 wretch to be:

Only my plague 1797 thus far I count my gain,

That she that makes me sin awards me pain 1798 .

142

Love is my sin and thy dear 1799 virtue hate,

Hate of my sin, grounded on sinful 1800 loving.

O, but with mine compare thou thine own state,

And thou shalt find it merits 1801 not reproving,

Or if it do, not from those lips of thine

That have profaned 1802 their scarlet ornaments

And sealed 1803 false bonds of love as oft as mine,

Robbed others’ beds’ revenues of their rents 1804 .

Be it 1805 lawful I love thee, as thou lov’st those

Whom thine eyes woo as mine importune 1806 thee.

Root 1807 pity in thy heart, that when it grows,

Thy pity may deserve to pitied be.

If thou dost seek to have what thou dost hide 1808 ,

By self-example 1809 mayst thou be denied.

143

Lo, as a careful 1810 housewife runs to catch

One of her feathered creatures 1811 broke away 1812 ,

Sets down her babe and makes all swift dispatch 1813

In pursuit of the thing she would have stay,

Whilst her neglected child holds her in chase 1814 ,

Cries to catch her whose busy care is bent

To follow that which flies before her face,

Not prizing 1815 her poor infant’s discontent:

So runn’st thou after that which flies from thee,

Whilst I, thy babe, chase thee afar behind.

But if thou catch thy hope, turn back to me,

And play the mother’s part, kiss me, be kind 1816 .

So will I pray that thou mayst have thy Will 1817 ,

If thou turn back and my loud crying still 1818 .

144 1819

Two loves 1820 I have of comfort and despair,

Which like two spirits do suggest 1821 me still 1822 :

The better angel is a man right fair 1823 ,

The worser spirit a woman coloured ill 1824 .

To win me soon to hell, my female evil

Tempteth my better angel from my side

And would corrupt my saint to be a devil,

Wooing his purity with her foul pride 1825 .

And whether that my angel be turned fiend 1826

Suspect I may, yet not directly tell;

But being both from me 1827 , both to each friend 1828 ,

I guess one angel in another’s hell 1829 .

Yet this shall I ne’er know, but live in doubt,

Till my bad angel fire my good one out 1830 .

145 1831

Those lips that Love’s own hand did make

Breathed forth the sound that said ‘I hate’

To me that languished for her sake.

But when she saw my woeful state,

Straight 1832 in her heart did mercy come,

Chiding that tongue that ever sweet

Was used in giving gentle doom 1833 ,

And 1834 taught it thus anew to greet:

‘I hate’ she altered with an end

That followed it as gentle day

Doth follow night, who like a fiend

From heaven to hell is flown away.

‘I hate’ from hate away she threw

And saved my life, saying ‘not you’.

146 1835

Poor soul, the centre of my sinful earth,

[ ] these rebel powers 1836 that thee array 1837 ,

Why dost thou pine 1838 within and suffer dearth 1839 ,

Painting thy outward walls so costly gay 1840 ?

Why so large cost, having so short a lease,

Dost thou upon thy fading mansion 1841 spend?

Shall worms, inheritors of this excess,

Eat up thy charge 1842 ? Is this thy body’s end?

Then, soul, live thou upon thy servant 1843 ’s loss

And let that pine to aggravate 1844 thy store,

Buy terms divine 1845 in selling hours of dross 1846 ,

Within be fed, without 1847 be rich no more:

So shalt thou feed on death 1848 , that feeds on men,

And death once dead, there’s no more dying then.

147

My love is as a fever, longing still 1849

For that which longer nurseth 1850 the disease,

Feeding on that which doth preserve the ill 1851 ,

Th’uncertain 1852 sickly appetite 1853 to please.

My reason, the physician to my love,

Angry that his prescriptions are not kept,

Hath left me and I desperate now approve 1854

Desire is death 1855 , which physic did except 1856 .

Past cure I am, now reason is past care 1857 ;

And frantic-mad with evermore 1858 unrest,

My thoughts and my discourse as madmen’s are,

At random from the truth 1859 vainly 1860 expressed.

For I have sworn thee fair 1861 and thought thee bright,

Who art as black 1862 as hell, as dark as night.

148

O me, what eyes hath love 1863 put in my head,

Which have no correspondence with true sight,

Or if they have, where is my judgement fled

That censures falsely 1864 what they see aright?

If that be fair whereon my false eyes dote,

What means the world to say it is not so?

If it be not, then love doth well denote

Love’s eye 1865 is not so true as all men’s ‘no’.

How can it? O, how can love’s eye be true,

That is so vexed 1866 with watching 1867 and with tears?

No marvel then though 1868 I mistake my view 1869 :

The sun itself sees not till heaven clears.

O cunning love, with tears thou keep’st me blind,

Lest eyes well-seeing thy foul faults 1870 should find.

149

Canst thou, O cruel, say I love thee not,

When I against myself with thee partake 1871 ?

Do I not think on thee, when I forgot

Am of 1872 myself, all tyrant 1873 for thy sake?

Who hateth thee that I do call my friend?

On whom frown’st thou that I do fawn upon?

Nay, if thou lour’st 1874 on me, do I not spend 1875

Revenge upon myself with present moan 1876 ?

What merit do I in myself respect 1877 ,

That is so proud thy service to despise 1878 ,

When all my best doth worship thy defect 1879 ,

Commanded by the motion of thine eyes?

But, love, hate on, for now I know thy mind:

Those that can see thou lov’st 1880 , and I am blind.

150

O, from what power hast thou this powerful might

With insufficiency 1881 my heart to sway 1882 ,

To make me give the lie to 1883 my true sight

And swear that brightness doth not grace the day 1884 ?

Whence hast thou this becoming of things ill 1885 ,

That in the very refuse of thy deeds 1886

There is such strength and warrantize 1887 of skill

That in my mind thy worst all best exceeds?

Who taught thee how to make me love thee more,

The more I hear and see just cause of hate?

O, though I love what others do abhor 1888 ,

With others thou shouldst not abhor my state.

If thy unworthiness raised love in me,

More worthy I to be beloved of thee.

151

Love is too young 1889 to know what conscience 1890 is,

Yet who knows not conscience is born of love?

Then, gentle cheater, urge not my amiss 1891 ,

Lest guilty of my faults thy sweet self prove.

For, thou betraying me 1892 , I do betray

My nobler part 1893 to my gross 1894 body’s treason 1895 .

My soul doth tell my body that he may

Triumph in love: flesh stays no further reason 1896 ,

But rising 1897 at thy name doth point out thee

As his triumphant prize 1898 . Proud of this pride 1899 ,

He is contented thy poor drudge 1900 to be,

To stand in thy affairs, fall 1901 by thy side.

No want 1902 of conscience hold 1903 it that I call

Her ‘love’ for whose dear love I rise and fall.

152

In loving thee thou know’st I am forsworn 1904 ,

But thou art twice forsworn to me love swearing 1905 :

In act 1906 thy bed-vow 1907 broke and new faith torn

In vowing new hate after new love bearing 1908 .

But why of two oaths’ breach do I accuse thee,

When I break twenty? I am perjured most,

For all my vows are oaths but to misuse 1909 thee,

And all my honest faith in thee is lost.

For I have sworn deep oaths of thy deep kindness 1910 ,

Oaths of thy love, thy truth, thy constancy,

And to enlighten 1911 thee gave eyes to blindness,

Or made them swear against the thing they see.

For I have sworn thee fair: more perjured eye 1912 ,

To swear against the truth so foul 1913 a lie.

153

Cupid 1914 laid by his brand and fell asleep.

A maid of Dian 1915 ’s this advantage found,

And his love-kindling fire did quickly steep 1916

In a cold valley-fountain of that ground 1917 ,

Which borrowed from this holy fire of love

A dateless 1918 lively heat, still to endure 1919 ,

And grew 1920 a seething bath, which yet men prove 1921

Against strange 1922 maladies a sovereign 1923 cure.

But at my mistress’ eye love’s brand new-fired 1924 ,

The boy 1925 for trial 1926 needs would 1927 touch my breast 1928 .

I, sick withal 1929 , the help of bath 1930 desired,

And thither hied 1931 , a sad distempered 1932 guest,

But found no cure: the bath for my help lies

Where Cupid got new fire — my mistress’ eyes.

154

The little Love-god 1933 lying once asleep

Laid by his side his heart-inflaming brand 1934 ,

Whilst many nymphs that vowed chaste life to keep

Came tripping by, but in her maiden hand

The fairest votary 1935 took up that fire

Which many legions of true hearts had warmed,

And so the general 1936 of hot desire

Was, sleeping, by a virgin hand disarmed.

This brand she quenched in a cool well by,

Which from love’s fire took heat perpetual,

Growing a bath and healthful remedy

For men diseased 1937 : but I, my mistress’ thrall 1938 ,

Came there for cure, and this by that I prove:

Love’s fire heats water, water cools not love.

NOTES

1 BEGETTER creator, father, author; could possibly mean ‘inspirer’

2 MR. W. H. often assumed to be the young man addressed in Sonnets 1–126, he has been variously identified – the most likely candidates are Henry Wriothesley, Earl of Southampton (to whom Venus and Adonis and Lucrece were dedicated) and William Herbert, Earl of Pembroke (to whom the First Folio was dedicated); however, MR. does not suggest an aristocrat, so it is possible that W. H. is a misprint for ‘W. S.’, and that this is the publisher’s dedication to the author

3 ETERNITY immortality

4 OUR EVER-LIVING POET Shakespeare (if a certain W. H. is the dedicatee)/God, via a pun on poet as ‘maker’ (if ‘W. H.’ should actually read ‘W. S.’)

5 ADVENTURER one who undertakes a commercial venture (i.e. the publisher)

6 T. T. Thomas Thorpe, the publisher of the sonnets

7 increase reproduction/offspring

8 That so that

9 tender young/loving

10 bear his memory be a reminder of him

11 thou in ordinary speech, ‘thou’ was generally more intimate than ‘you’ (like tu in French), but it was also the conventional pronoun for poetic address

12 contracted betrothed (plays on the sense of ‘diminished’, i.e. by not having children)

13 eyes perhaps puns on ‘I’s’

14 Feed’st … fuel i.e. like a candle, the young man feeds his flame (i.e. his life) with his own body

15 fresh young, lively, blooming

17 gaudy bright (without connotations of vulgarity)

18 bud an image of youth, also of virginity; may additionally refer to the glans of the penis

19 content happiness/contents (i.e. potential children)

20 tender churl a paradox: he is both loving/sensitive (tender ) and mean/boorish (a churl )

22 niggarding being mean, stingy; another paradox – he wastes by being sparing

USER’S GUIDE

Titles follow the First Folio, where there are sometimes minor variations of nomenclature between contents list, title at head of play and running header – our default preference is the title at the head of the play (thus, for example, Love’s Labour’s Lost and The Tragedy of Cymbeline as opposed to the contents list’s Love’s Labour Lost and Cymbeline King of Britain) . Some of the plays were originally staged with different titles (e.g. The Second Part of Henry the Sixth began its life as The First Part of the Contention betwixt the two famous Houses of York and Lancaster, and Henry the Eighth seems to have been called All is True ).

Lists of Parts are supplied in the First Folio for only six plays, so these are predominantly editorial, arranged by groups of character (thus for A Midsummer Night’s Dream , the court, the genteel lovers, the artisans, the fairies). Capitals indicate that part of the name which is used for speech headings in the script (thus ‘PROSPERO , the right Duke of Milan’ and ‘Sir John FALSTAFF’).

Locations are provided by the Folio for only two plays (The Tempest is set on ‘an uninhabited island’ and Measure for Measure in Vienna). Eighteenth-century editors, working in an age of elaborately realistic stage sets, were the first to provide detailed locations. Given that Shakespeare wrote for a bare stage and often an imprecise sense of place, we have relegated locations to the explanatory notes at the foot of the page, where they are given at the beginning of each scene where the imaginary location is different from the one before. We have emphasized broad geographical settings rather than specifics of the kind that suggest anachronistically realistic staging. Thus Sicilia and Bohemia or court and country in The Winter’s Tale , Venice and Cyprus in Othello, not usually such locations as ‘another room in the palace’. Indications of movement from outdoors to indoors are given where appropriate.

Act and Scene Divisions were provided in the Folio (they were absent from the Quartos published in Shakespeare’s lifetime). Sometimes, however, they were erroneous or omitted; corrections and additions supplied by editorial tradition are indicated by square brackets. Five-act division is based on a classical model, and act breaks provided the opportunity to replace the candles in the indoor Blackfriars playhouse which the King’s Men used after 1608, but Shakespeare did not necessarily think in terms of a five-part structure of dramatic composition. The Folio convention is that a scene ends when the stage is empty. Nowadays, partly under the influence of film, we tend to consider a scene to be a dramatic unit that ends with either a change of imaginary location or a significant passage of time within the narrative. Shakespeare’s fluidity of composition accords well with this convention, so in addition to act and scene numbers we provide a running scene count at the beginning of each new scene. Where there is a scene break caused by a momentary bare stage, but the location does not change and extra time does not pass, we use the convention running scene continues . There is inevitably a degree of editorial judgement in making such calls, but the system is very valuable in suggesting the pace of the plays (it reveals, for instance, the great compression of Othello ).

Speaker’s Names are often inconsistent in Folio. We have regularized speech headings, but retained an element of deliberate inconsistency in entry directions, in order to give the flavour of Folio.

Verse is indicated by lines that do not run to the right margin and by capitalization of the first letter of each line. The Folio printers sometimes set verse as prose, and vice versa (either out of misunderstanding or for reasons of space). We have silently corrected in such cases, although in some instances there is ambiguity, in which case we have leaned towards the preservation of Folio layout. Folio sometimes uses contraction (‘turnd’ rather than ‘turned’) to indicate whether or not the final ‘-ed’ of a past participle is sounded, an area where there is variation for the sake of the five-beat iambic pentameter rhythm. We use the convention of a grave accent to indicate sounding (thus ‘turnèd’ would be two syllables), but would urge actors not to overstress. In cases where one speaker ends with a verse half-line and the next begins with the other half of the pentameter, editors since the late eighteenth century have indented the second line. We have abandoned this convention, since the Folio does not use it, and nor did actors’ cues in the Shakespearean theatre. An exception is made when the second speaker actively interrupts or completes the first speaker’s sentence.

Spelling is modernized, but older forms are occasionally maintained where necessary for rhythm or aural effect. For instance, ‘y’are’ is generally modernized to ‘you’re’, but the old form is retained in Regan’s ‘I know you are of her bosom … Y’are. I know’t.’

Punctuation in Shakespeare’s time was as much rhetorical as grammatical. ‘Colon’ was originally a term for a unit of thought in an argument. The semi-colon was a new unit of punctuation (some of the Quartos lack them altogether). We have modernized punctuation throughout, but have given more weight to Folio punctuation than many editors, since, though not Shakespearean, it reflects the usage of his period. In particular, we have used the colon far more than many editors: it is exceptionally useful as a way of indicating the way in which many Shakespearean speeches unfold clause by clause in a developing argument that gives the illusion of enacting the process of thinking in the moment. We have also kept in mind the origin of punctuation in classical times as a means of assisting the actor and orator: the comma suggests the briefest of pauses for breath, the colon a middling one and a full stop or period a longer pause. Semi-colons, by contrast, belong to an era of punctuation that was only just coming in during Shakespeare’s time and that is coming to an end now: we have accordingly only used them where they occur in our copy-texts (and not always then). Dashes are sometimes used for parenthetical interjections where the Folio has brackets. They are also used for interruptions and changes in train of thought. Where a change of addressee occurs within a speech, we have used a dash preceded by a full stop (or occasionally another form of punctuation). Often the identity of the respective addressees is obvious from the context. When it is not, this has been indicated in a marginal stage direction.

Entrances and Exits are fairly thorough in Folio, which has accordingly been followed as faithfully as possible. Where characters are omitted or corrections are necessary, this is indicated by square brackets (e.g. ‘[and Attendants ]’). Exit is sometimes silently normalized to Exeunt and Manet anglicized to ‘remains’. We trust Folio positioning of entrances and exits to a greater degree than most editors: thus, for instance, we follow Folio in having the other characters enter in the middle of Petruchio’s speech to Kate at Taming of the Shrew , 2.1.268–81, making the latter part of the speech into a kind of game for the benefit of the onstage audience (an interpretation that strongly shaped Greg Doran’s RSC production).

Other Stage Directions such as stage business, asides and indications of addressee are rare in Folio. Modern editions usually mingle editorial directions with original Folio and Quarto ones, sometimes marking interventions by means of square brackets. We have sought to distinguish what could be described as directorial interventions of this kind from Folio-style directions (either original or supplied) by placing the former in the right margin in a different typeface. There is a degree of subjectivity about which directions are of which kind, but the procedure is intended as a reminder to the reader and the actor that Shakespearean stage directions are often dependent upon editorial inference alone and are not set in stone. We also depart from editorial tradition in sometimes admitting uncertainty and thus printing permissive stage directions, such as an Aside ?(often a line may be equally effective as an aside or a direct address – it is for each production or reading to make its own decision) or a may exit or a piece of business placed between arrows to indicate that it may occur at various different moments within a scene.

Explanatory Notes at the foot of each page explain allusions and gloss obsolete and difficult words, confusing phraseology, occasional major textual cruces, and so on. Particular attention is given to non-standard usage, bawdy innuendo and technical terms (e.g. legal and military language). Where more than one sense is given, commas indicate shades of related meaning, slashes alternative or double meanings.

Textual Notes at the end of each play indicate major departures from the Folio. They take the following form: the reading of our text is given in bold and its source given after an equals sign, with ‘Q’ indicating that it derives from the principal Quarto and ‘Ed’ that it derives from the editorial tradition. The rejected Folio (‘F’) reading is then given. A selection of Quarto variants and plausible unadopted editorial readings are also included. Thus, for example, at Hamlet , 3.4.180, ’ bloat = Ed. F = blunt. Q = blowt’, indicates that we have adopted the editorial reading’bloat’ where Folio has ‘blunt’ and Quarto has ‘blowt’.

‘Key Facts’ boxes at the beginning of each work give information on plot, sources, proportion of verse and prose, textual issues, authorial attribution when doubtful or collaborative, date of composition and first performance. They also include lists of substantial parts (characters who speak more than about forty lines) in descending order of size. Since the definition of a line spoken is variable (because of prose and half-lines), figures are given in terms of a percentage of the whole, the number of speeches delivered and the number of scenes the character is onstage. These lists make it possible to distinguish between works that are more ensemble-based (e.g. A Midsummer Night’s Dream and 2 Henry IV ) and those that are dominated by a small number of characters (e.g. Othello and 1 Henry IV ). Though there is a degree of imprecision about these lists as a result of editorial interventions, they offer much valuable information – for instance that Sir Toby Belch has the largest part in Twelfth Night and that only six lead characters so dominate their world that their part is over three times the length of anyone else’s in the play (Hamlet, Richard III, Timon, Macbeth, Prospero, Henry V).
